## The Background

Rise of Mortals is a heroic fantasy roleplay system focused on player creativity, and on making every choice impactful and unique.

The setting of this document is the planet of Drasil, where magic and monsters are common knowledge. Any specific characters or locations referenced in this book belong to this setting and no other. The system is not setting-agnostic — it needs certain pieces of lore to explain how the game works — but you can build almost any fantasy setting of your own without changing much.

## Before We Start

This system is meant to give players almost unlimited creativity in what they can do or try to do — but that takes trust from both sides. If a player tries to cheese the system, using its open-ended nature just to "win" (meaning: overcome challenges without any effort or a satisfying outcome), it is the Qg's job to stop them. And it goes both ways: if the Qg abuses their power in a way that makes players uncomfortable, it is the players' responsibility to call them out.

**The first rule:** the Quest giver's word is final, and their interpretation of the rules is the correct one for as long as they are the Qg.

Players are asked to respect the Qg, who invested time and effort into creating an adventure — and to play by their rulings, even when official sources contradict them.

The Qg, in turn, is asked to put the fun and safety of their players above all else. We highly advise warning players in advance about any sensitive topics your campaign may include.

And if a player is not having fun, do not ignore it. If someone is not having fun, the game has failed its purpose. Ask what you can do to help.

**Rise of Mortals does not support player-versus-player play.** Talent trees are balanced to work with each other, not against each other. The Qg is advised to prevent PvP at the table and to resolve conflicts between players peacefully.

We suggest playing with people you trust, and keeping an open mind. Rise of Mortals welcomes everyone into this wonderful roleplaying experience, and we hope you get to enjoy your many adventures.

**Now go, heroes — to the many trials awaiting you, to the treasure and friendships the future holds, and to a world ever expanding.**

## Terminology

**Qg (Quest giver):** the person who runs the game — they control the story, play the side characters and villains of the setting, and rule on anything this book does not cover.

**Character / creature:** all heroes, villains, enemies and monsters are considered characters and creatures. Almost every feature affects them identically unless specified otherwise.

**Heroes:** the player characters of Rise of Mortals — the ones the adventure focuses on.

**Enemies:** the foes the heroes fight. Some are intelligent, some act only on instinct. Most enemies have enough self-preservation to run away when they don't stand a chance to win — but monsters will fight until the very end.

**Villains:** a more powerful enemy or monster — the creator of the main conflict of the story. Villains may be given special abilities that break certain rules, to show just how powerful they are. We recommend making one person or organization the main antagonist of the story.

**Attribute:** one of the 7 core traits of every character: power, speed, endurance, willpower, intellect, sense and charisma. Each represents a different side of your character, and almost every roll in the game is based on one of them.

**The 6 stats:** values calculated from your attributes. They are static — they only change when you improve an attribute, or through equipment and talents:

- **Unphased:** a character's ability to take damage and still operate at maximum capacity.
- **Resilience:** the ability to endure harsh damage and keep going. A character with high resilience can take heavy punishment without being knocked out.
- **Evasion:** how hard it is to land a direct hit on a character — the quick footwork of a nimble assassin, or a bandit hidden deep in the forest.
- **Damage reduction:** a character's ability to absorb damage without effect — heavy armor, or simply the hardened scales of a dragon.
- **Mana:** the energy that fuels magical effects — casting spells and more. It represents the mana a character gained through training, was gifted at birth, or a combination of the two.
- **Movement:** how far a character can travel on their turn.

**Ability test / check:** any roll that adds one of the core attributes to its result — a power roll to hit with a weapon, or a speed check to dodge falling rocks.

**Target:** a creature or object you want to affect. Unless specifically stated otherwise, you can always target yourself.

**Ability:** an umbrella term for all spells, attacks, talents and features granted by equipment.

**Negative conditions:** restrained, prone, intimidated, taunted, blinded, unaware, weakened and poisoned — as well as lingering effects certain attacks inflict, such as shock, frost, ranks of bleed and continual damage. If any of these applies to you, you have been inflicted with a negative condition.

## Basic Rules

### The Rolling System

Rise of Mortals uses its rolling system to determine the outcomes of uncertain events. For example: Alex is playing a warrior and wants to see if her overhead slash hits the dragon, and John is playing a wizard and wants to know how much damage his lightning bolt just dealt to the cave trolls.

#### When to roll?

Only when the outcome of an action is unknown. If Alex is playing a mighty warrior who can cut down trees in a single swing, that warrior can probably clear a 5-meter jump without much difficulty — no roll needed. John's wizard, far less physically capable, might have to roll in the same situation.

Knowledge works the same way. If it is reasonable to assume John's wizard learned the basics of mana during his years of training, there is no reason to hide that information behind a roll. Alex's hero, on the other hand, is unlikely to know it.

**Advantage and disadvantage.** When the situation calls for it, a character gains an advantage or a disadvantage:

- **Advantage:** roll one additional die. If your roll uses several types of dice, add one die of the largest type you have.
- **Disadvantage:** the maximum success count of your roll is reduced by 1.

#### What determines success and failure?

Whenever you roll a die of any size, every result of **4 or above** counts as a success. On a d4, only a 4 succeeds; on a d10, everything from 4 to 10 does. And a 4 rolled on a d4 and a 7 rolled on a d10 are both worth exactly **1 success**.

**Important note:** if you roll the maximum possible value on a die, you may roll one additional die of the same type.

*Example: you roll 3d6 and get 1, 5 and 6. The 6 is the maximum possible — so you roll one more d6.*

The total number of successes you rolled is your **success score**. After determining it, compare it to the **test score**, which is set by the Qg or by the stats of the character opposing you:

- If your success score is **equal to or higher** than the test score, you succeed.
- If the test score is higher, you fail.

*In other words: on a tie, the aggressor beats the defender.*

**A note on halving:** whenever a rule asks you to halve a value in Rise of Mortals, round up.

#### How do I know how many dice I roll?

Every roll is different, but a simple example shows how the dice come together.

**Step 1 — declare the action.** Alex, our favorite warrior, is trying to lift a boulder that blocks the party's path.

**Step 2 — determine the attribute.** Alex is lifting the boulder so her friends can move forward — so she is obviously using her power.

**Step 3 — check your talents.** Alex has the Strong armed talent, which aids in lifting, grappling, pushing and pulling checks. Its main use is grappling, but it is perfectly reasonable to apply its bonus die to this roll too.

**Step 4 — check your items.** Alex has no items that would help in this situation.

**Step 5 — determine any other modifiers.** John the wizard decides to help lift the boulder, so we add one of his power dice to the check.

*(When another character assists, they add only **one** die to the roll. So if Alex, with her 2d10 power, were helping John, she would add just 1d10 to his roll.)*

**Step 6 — add it all up, roll, and compare.** Alex's power is 2d10, Strong armed gives her another 1d8, and John's help adds his 1d4. The Qg sets the test score of this action at 3. Alex rolls:

- a 3 and an 8 on her d10s,
- a 5 on the d8,
- a 4 on the d4.

That is **3 successes** against a test score of 3 — thanks to John's help, Alex lifts the boulder and the party continues on its journey.

*This may look like a lot, but most of the time the player knows all their modifiers before declaring the action, and the Qg knows the test score almost immediately. If this is the third time Alex and John have lifted a boulder together, everyone at the table already knows what to roll.*

#### Adding and subtracting successes

Many effects add or remove successes from a roll, and they stack. If two separate instances of "reduce one success from the roll" apply to the same roll, you reduce two successes from it. The same logic applies to multiple effects adding successes to the same roll.

#### A note on modifiers: the five levers

Every bonus and penalty in this book pulls one of five levers. Knowing which is which keeps the math clean:

1. **Bonus dice** *("+1d8 to the roll")* — more dice in your hand. Dice add *potential*: a bonus die can still roll a 1.
2. **Success modifiers** *("+1 success", "−1 success")* — applied **after** the dice land. These are guaranteed: no luck involved.
3. **Test score modifiers** *("the test score is increased by 1")* — the bar itself moves. Your roll doesn't change; what counts as *enough* does.
4. **Evasion modifiers** *("+1 evasion")* — the same lever, for attacks: evasion **is** the test score of an attack roll.
5. **Damage modifiers** *("+1 damage", damage reduction)* — applied last, only once a hit is confirmed. They never touch the roll itself.

The order of every roll: gather your dice (lever 1) → roll, count successes → apply success modifiers (lever 2) → compare against the test score or evasion, as modified (levers 3–4) → on a success, resolve damage and its modifiers (lever 5). A roll can never drop below **0 successes** — and if a test score drops to **0**, no roll is needed at all: you simply succeed.

When in doubt about which lever an effect pulls, read it literally: "to the roll" means dice, "successes" means successes, and anything naming evasion or a test score moves the bar.

#### What types of rolls are there?

There are a few types of rolls, or "checks" — the two words are used interchangeably in this book.

**Hostile roll:** a roll made to harm, impair or intercept another creature. The most common hostile roll is an attack, made to deal damage.

**Resist roll:** a roll made to ward off damage or negative effects — escaping a grapple, or avoiding falling rocks.

**Resisting damage:** if a damaging effect calls for its targets to roll, each success reduces the damage taken by 1. If the effect carries a damage tag or an additional effect (such as a dragon's poison breath), the target must take the damage all the way down to **0** to avoid the tag or the additional effect.

**Check roll:** a roll made when you are neither hurting another creature nor resisting one — gathering information, lying, or finding food. When a feature says it adds dice to "all [attribute] checks", it refers to this type of roll only.

### Attributes

In Rise of Mortals, each character has 7 attributes, plus 6 other stats calculated from them.

The 7 attributes are: **power, speed, endurance, intellect, sense, willpower** (or will) **and charisma**. Each attribute is represented by dice, from a d4 to a d12, and the number next to the die shows how many of them you roll. For example, 2d6 in charisma means you add two six-sided dice to any roll related to charisma.

The 6 stats are: **resilience, evasion, damage reduction, unphased, mana and movement**. They are calculated from your attributes and are static — they only change when you improve an attribute, or through equipment and talents.

#### What does each attribute do?

Each of the 7 attributes covers a different kind of activity — some physical, some mental. Almost every roll in the game is related to one of them.

**Power:** physical strength and ferocity — and how much damage your attacks deal. Power is commonly used for attack rolls, for restraining or tripping foes with your weight or strength, for pushing, pulling, lifting or dragging heavy objects, and for feats like hauling yourself up by your arms or swimming against a current.

**Speed:** dexterity, reflexes, and just generally how fast you can be. Speed is used for intricate and quick actions — sprinting, pulling off an acrobatic maneuver, making a quick thrust with your weapon, or grabbing a ledge while falling.

**Endurance:** stamina, and your ability to keep at a task over a long stretch of time — climbing a mountainside for hours, swimming for a whole day, carrying heavy loads over long distances. Endurance also represents your body's resistance to temperature, sickness and poison.

**Intellect:** the ability to recall knowledge, control mana at the basic level, and come up with new ideas. Intellect rolls cover remembering or researching knowledge, identifying spells, and even figuring out someone's plan or the workings of a complex machine.

**Sense:** spatial awareness, attention to small details, the ability to move silently and discreetly, and the ability to read the intent of other creatures. Common sense rolls: hiding, examining your surroundings, or telling whether a creature is lying.

**Willpower:** the ability to resist fear and keep your cool. Willpower resists almost all mental effects — intimidation, mind control and the like — and also represents determination and focus.

**Charisma:** the ability to influence others — through smart persuasion, rough intimidation, intricate deception, or any other way you can think of. Charisma is used in negotiations, and whenever a creature wants to assert dominance in a scene.

#### Dice size and dice number

When you improve an attribute, you will usually see one of two phrases: **+1 dice size** or **+1 dice**. These refer to the two parts of every attribute — how many sides each die has, and how many dice you roll.

**Dice size** is the number of sides: a d6 has six, a d10 has ten. Because a success is a roll of 4 or above, bigger dice succeed more often.

- **+1 dice size:** the die becomes one step bigger, following the ladder **d4 → d6 → d8 → d10 → d12**. A d12 is the maximum — applying +1 dice size to a d12 does nothing.

**Dice number** is the count on the left: it shows how many dice you roll when using that attribute.

- **+1 dice:** you simply roll one more die of the same type. If you have 1d10 in power and gain +1 dice to power, you now roll 2d10.

Your dice size can never exceed a d12, and your dice number can never exceed 6.

#### The 6 stats and how to improve them

The 6 stats calculated from your attributes are resilience, movement, evasion, damage reduction, mana and unphased. They are static, and only change when you upgrade one of your attributes — or through better equipment and talents.

**Resilience** — endurance. A creature's ability to take damage without fainting. Every creature has a base resilience of 2, and gains **+1 resilience per endurance die — or +3 per die if the endurance die is a d8 or above**. When your resilience is reduced to 0, you become Ashen (see: [Death, and the path of ash](#death-and-the-path-of-ash)).

**Movement** — speed. How far your character can travel on their turn. Your base movement is **10 meters — or 15 if your speed die is a d8 or above** — and every speed die beyond the first grants **+3 meters**.

**Evasion** — speed. Your character's ability to avoid direct damage, be it quick footwork or a solid defense. You gain **+1 evasion per speed die, and +1 more if your speed die is a d10 or above**. Your evasion is the test score whenever you are targeted by an attack.

**Mana** — charisma and intellect. The energy in all living things of Rise of Mortals, used to cast spells and empower certain abilities. You gain **4 mana per charisma die — or 7 per die if your charisma die is a d8 or above — plus 3 mana per intellect die**. When you cast a spell using mana manipulation, you spend mana to cast it.

**Unphased** — willpower. How much damage a creature can take while still operating at maximum capacity. You start with **1 unphased, and gain +1 per willpower die — or +2 per die if your willpower die is a d10 or above**.

Damage always reduces your unphased first; only once your unphased is at 0 (or when an attack specifically ignores unphased) does damage reduce your resilience. If you have taken more damage than your unphased stat, you become heavily wounded (see: [Combat](#combat)). You regain all unphased after each fight.

**Damage reduction** — equipment and talents. Your ability to shrug off part of incoming damage, written as a negative number: a damage reduction of −2 means every hit deals 2 less damage to you. This stat is unique: it is not calculated from any attribute, and **the damage reduction you gain from equipment cannot go beyond −2** — though this ceiling rises as your renown grows (see: [The talent system](#the-talent-system)). Bonuses from talents and from your rank stack past that cap — a fencer in good armor can reach −3 or −4.

#### Size (heh)

A character's size determines the dimensions they fill in combat. This mostly matters in adventures that run combat on a square grid (which is assumed).

A character's size is their height and length. A dragon 10 meters tall takes up a 10-by-10 square of spaces on the battlefield. It is assumed a character's footprint is proportional to their height: a character between 1 and 2 meters tall is size 2 and takes up a 2×2 square; a character between 4 and 5 meters tall is size 5.

- To clear something up: a character's combat size represents the threat area others can't enter while fighting you — not that the character is actually 2 meters wide.

Difference in size affects the following:

- If you are grappling a smaller creature (or a smaller creature is grappling you), they suffer −1 success when trying to escape your grapple using power.
- You gain +1 success to escape a restraining effect if its AoE is smaller than your size.
- When a smaller creature pushes you, you are pushed 1 meter less for every point of size difference (to a minimum of 1).
- You cannot grapple a creature that is twice your size.
- You may stand in a space one size smaller than you. This is called squeezing, and you count as prone for as long as you squeeze.

### Building a Character

To start playing Rise of Mortals, every player who is not the Qg must create a Hero. There are six steps in the process.

#### 1) Create a concept

This step matters most for new players, or players who can't decide what they want to play. Think of a fantasy archetype or a character you like, and take inspiration from them — but don't just copy a character. Don't make Aragorn from The Lord of the Rings; a noble-turned-veteran nature survivor who is proficient in many weapons, however, is a perfectly valid concept.

To bring the concept to life, think about the hero's personality, or start sketching a backstory. You can even build a character around one cool talent you like.

**Important:** a good concept should be achievable with your starting talents, and grow from there through the talent trees — unless the Qg lets you start with extra talent points.

#### 2) Choose your race

This step is important: some races have racial abilities that can strengthen certain playstyles or open up new ones.

There are **12 races** in Rise of Mortals, plus **3 additional options available to every race** (see Racial modifications below).

**Human:** the original mortal race, still embodying the unbreakable spirit that once helped defeat the dead god.

**Elves:** the shortest race of mortals in Drasil. Their nimble nature makes them ideal messengers between cities — a highly dangerous and respected job.

**Ogres:** large mortals who recover from the most devastating wounds quickly. Some legends say there are ogres who regrow lost limbs.

**Rock gnomes:** mortals created by infusing elementals into empty shells of minerals. They are extremely rare, and prized for their nature.

**Devil-kin:** mortals infused with demonic essence at an early age. They may grow charcoal skin, and eyes the color of ice, fire or poison.

**Hybrids:** part human, part animal, these mortals adapted to life away from the big cities, living in small nomadic communities suited to their animal side.

**Mound folk:** a tribal culture with a special connection to the earth. Myths say they were created by the spirit of Teragamalt, the great tree.

**Giant kin:** descendants of the union between mortals and giants. They inherited the giants' connection to arcane magic, and are able to scribe ancient runes.

**Seraphim:** those who bear the skin of an angel. Whether it was gifted to them, they were born with it, or it was grafted onto them in a ritual doesn't matter — what matters is the angel's ability to disguise their horrific appearance with illusions.

**Wyvern Born:** bearers of the draconic spark, these mortals carry the mark of ancient magic. Their gaze is piercing, their movements swift, and the wind always seems to be at their side — their nature echoes the first step in the evolution of the great dragons.

**Icewings:** their wings let them glide through the air, and their bodies bear the marks of an ancient power. Cities are foreign to them — they are only at home among ice, wind and high places.

**Stormblessed:** heirs of the sky dragons and the lightning they carry. Their appearance mirrors the storms that once ruled the heavens — electricity runs in their blood, and thunder seems to answer their call.

**Racial modifications.** Any race can be born with one of these. A modification **added to** your race's base ability with something unique (full rules in the Races chapter):

- **Treeblessed:** those whose souls are bound to the world tree and the spirits of nature. Their bodies answer to the rhythms of the earth, and the forests themselves treat their presence with respect.
- **Manifested:** their very essence is saturated with magic, showing through in even the simplest of actions. These beings seem woven from energy — and it answers their will faster than anyone else's.
- **Flame Forged:** shaped by flame, they carry an echo of the departed god of fire. Their strength is in heat, their spirit is in flame — and every step they take leaves only ash behind.

#### 3) Choose your attribute dice

For each of the seven attributes (power, speed, endurance, willpower, intellect, sense and charisma) you decide what die represents it. Distribute them as follows:

- two attributes at **d8**,
- three attributes at **d6**,
- two attributes at **d4**.

Each attribute starts with a single die. For example, if your attribute is represented by a d8, you add 1d8 to every roll of that attribute.

#### 4) Choose a background

A hero's background determines their early training. Create your own: think of where your hero comes from and what shaped them, then choose one attribute that reflects it — that attribute gains **+1 dice** (one more die, not a bigger one).

You may also agree with the Qg on a few trinkets that fit your background — items of sentimental rather than mechanical value. Your actual starting equipment is handled separately (see: [Equipment](#equipment)).

#### 5) Choose your starting talents

You gain **6 talent points**. You may spread them across any talent trees you like, with one restriction:

- You cannot acquire a level 3 talent at character creation, in any way.

Talent points don't have to be spent on talents alone — you can also invest them directly into your hero's attributes, growing stronger instead of learning something new (see: [The talent system](#the-talent-system)).

The full list of talents is at the end of this book.

#### 6) Your stats

After the first five steps, you have everything you need. Calculate your stats from the attributes you chose, and figure out your hero's starting equipment. Every hero starts with equipment of **standard quality** — though you may choose to start with poor quality gear instead, whether for roleplay reasons or to enable certain playstyles (the equipment list is in the Equipment chapter).

#### Tips, and a character example

To make character creation easier, here is an example of building a hero from scratch — following the six steps above.

**Character example: Ben the Bandit.** This hero will be a classic sharpshooter of the group: a stealthy, perceptive skirmisher who strikes from where nobody is looking.

**1) The concept.** We want a ranged, stealth-leaning character — but "a bandit" alone is boring. After 10–15 minutes of brainstorming: Ben was born to a farming family and had a good childhood, but the family farm couldn't feed everyone, so Ben took to the road — and to relieving rich caravans of their coin. A talented shot with a good heart and questionable methods.

**2) The race.** Ben is a human — the unbreakable spirit fits someone who chose the hard road and kept smiling. Humans gain +1 unphased and +1d8 to all willpower, intellect and charisma resist rolls. Ben's size is 2.

**3) The attribute dice.** Ben is a very sneaky guy and a talented sharpshooter, so his d8s go to **sense** and **speed**. Willpower, endurance and intellect take the d6s — and power with charisma get the d4s. Ben is not winning arm-wrestling contests or charming courts; he doesn't need to.

**4) The background.** Ben's farming past shaped his hands and his eye: we give the background's **+1 dice to speed**, the attribute of his ranged craft. Speed becomes 2d8. As trinkets, Ben carries a worn family sling and a lucky copper coin — the first thing he ever "earned" on the road.

**5) The starting talents.** Ben gets 6 talent points, and spends them like this:

- **The hunter** — levels 1 and 2 (*tracker*, *on the move*) — **2 points**. A life outdoors: Ben tracks prey and moves fast.
- **The sniper** — levels 1 and 2 (*Steady aim*, *far shot*) — **2 points**. The core of the concept: patient, precise shots.
- **The slayer** — level 1 (*skulker*) — **1 point**. Striking from where nobody is looking.
- The last point goes into **stats**: we raise two attributes by one dice size — **endurance d6 → d8** (farm work builds a body that keeps going) and **willpower d6 → d8** (a sniper's patience). As a rookie, Ben cannot raise anything beyond a d8 — and he is already there.

**6) Ben's stats.** Now we calculate everything:

| | |
|---|---|
| **Power** | 1d4 |
| **Speed** | 2d8 |
| **Endurance** | 1d8 |
| **Intellect** | 1d6 |
| **Sense** | 1d8 |
| **Willpower** | 1d8 |
| **Charisma** | 1d4 |

| | |
|---|---|
| **Resilience** | 5 *(2 base + 3 per endurance die at d8 or above)* |
| **Movement** | 18 m *(15 base at speed d8 or above, +3 for the second die)* |
| **Evasion** | 2 *(+1 per speed die)* |
| **Mana** | 7 *(4 per charisma die + 3 per intellect die)* |
| **Unphased** | 3 *(1 base +1 per willpower die, +1 human)* |
| **Damage reduction** | 0 |

| | |
|---|---|
| **Race** | Human (size 2) |
| **Racial ability** | +1d8 to willpower, intellect and charisma resist rolls |
| **Talents** | The hunter 2, The sniper 2, The slayer 1 |

Ben is ready for the road: he tracks his mark for days (*tracker*, *on the move*), settles into a good position (*skulker*), takes his time (*Steady aim*) — and rarely needs a second shot.

## Roleplay

This chapter will cover:

- Roleplaying: the basics
- Social interactions
- Side characters: friendly and hostile
- Rolling in roleplay
- Advice for roleplay
- Out of combat rolls

### Roleplaying: the basics

Most of your time in Rise of Mortals will be spent out of combat — traveling, camping, exploring old ruins, and other similar activities in and out of urban areas. It is expected, and even recommended, to engage in social interactions during most of these activities.

Roleplaying can be anything from idle banter with side characters or the other heroes, to emotionally driven moments between two characters. And remember: even if your character is alone, you can still roleplay. Describing your character's actions and thoughts is good roleplaying too — and it is a great way in if you are not comfortable acting out scenes with other people.

While roleplaying, try to imagine how your character would think, act and speak in that situation. If you play a smart and cunning thief who always has a plan, think carefully about your actions and avoid doing stupid things without a reason — if you want to keep playing that type of character. On the other side of things, your strong but simple warrior can still come up with battle tactics or help solve puzzles — just frame it in a way that fits the character: arriving at a smart idea through a train of thought that makes no sense, or getting one detail wrong that a more intelligent character would not.

The most important thing in roleplay is that everyone feels safe to share and express themselves. Before the adventure starts, decide together whether there are borders you don't cross or topics you don't bring up.

### Social interactions

Social interactions are scenes where the heroes interact with each other or with side characters. They are a core part of the Rise of Mortals experience — you can run whole adventures built on social interactions alone.

A social interaction doesn't necessarily have a goal that ends the scene, like combat does. Let the conversation flow naturally until it reaches an end point. Some interactions do have a goal — even if not everyone involved knows about it.

**What can I do in social interactions?**

Basically? Talk. But depending on the context, you may want to deceive, convince, intimidate, or take any other action that would change the course of the scene. When you take one of these "social actions", the Qg may ask for a roll — if there is a chance the other side won't be affected.

*Example: if your 2-meter-tall warrior with a greataxe intimidates a drunken peasant, no roll is needed — the Qg simply acts out the poor man's reaction. But if the same warrior threatens an armed guard, the Qg should call for a roll to see whether the guard takes the threat seriously or stands his ground.*

**Can I take non-social actions during a social interaction?**

Yes, absolutely. But be careful: if you make a hostile move against a creature, the situation can turn into a combat scene.

### Side characters

Side character is the name for every character or creature that is not a villain, hero or enemy — anything from an old shopkeeper to the tired monarch currently in power. Side characters are mostly friendly or neutral towards the heroes, but some will be negative (even if not strictly hostile) towards certain heroes or other side characters.

**A note for Qgs:** a detailed side character must have something to do with the heroes. If they are just "cool", with no connection to the characters or the plot, the players may never even interact with them. Think about what purpose a character serves in the story before you build them in detail.

- **Friendly side characters:** shopkeepers, traveling merchants, friendly nobles, or just a friendly face on the street.
- **Hostile side characters:** those with a motivation against the heroes — they will likely fight the heroes, or take advantage of them and other side characters.

### Rolling in roleplay

When any creature wants to do something notable with an uncertain outcome, the Qg may call for a roll. In roleplay this should be rarer than in combat: out of combat, let players do things that would normally require a roll — climb the side of a house, swim across a river — without rolling, as long as the action isn't too drastic. When a roll is needed, it follows the normal rules from The Rolling System, with one addition.

When you try to convince, intimidate, deceive or otherwise use your charisma against another creature, you are rolling against a test score based on the circumstances. **The base test score is 1** — the score for an ordinary creature with no other factors at play. The creature's attitude and circumstances modify it from there. That drunken peasant from earlier? Test score 1 — which is exactly why no roll was needed. The armed guard stacks *under someone's command* on top — and, if he sizes you up favorably, *stronger than you* as well:

| Attitude | Deceive | Intimidate | Convince |
|---|---|---|---|
| Friendly | — | — | −1 |
| Brave | — | +1 | — |
| Cautious | +1 | — | — |
| Hostile | — | +1 | +2 |
| Neutral | — | — | — |
| Cowardly | — | −2 | −1 |
| Gullible | −1 | — | −1 |

*(— means the test score is simply based on the creature, unmodified by this attitude.)*

- A creature can hold more than one attitude at a time, but cannot hold two or more of **friendly, hostile or neutral** at the same time.
- A creature cannot be **brave and cowardly** at the same time, or **gullible and cautious** at the same time.
- A creature can hold conflicting attitudes towards different targets — cautious with one creature and gullible with another, even in the same room.

**Circumstances:**

- **Under someone's command:** convincing this creature to willingly go against their superior's wishes is harder — the test score to convince or intimidate them into conflicting with their orders is **raised by 1**.
- **Stronger than you:** if the creature is sure they are stronger than you, the test score to intimidate them is **raised by 2**.
- **Owes you something:** if a creature is indebted to you in any way, the test score to convince them to act as repayment of that debt is **reduced by 2**.
- **You owe them:** if you owe the creature something and try to intimidate or convince them to help you, the test score is **raised by 1**.
- **Admires you:** the test score of all checks to deceive, convince or intimidate the creature is **reduced by 1**.
- **Trusts you:** the test score to deceive this creature is **reduced by 2**, and the test score to convince them to help you is **reduced by 1**.
- **Out of options:** if the creature knows that complying with your request brings a serious negative consequence for them, the test score is **raised by 2**.

**A note on intimidation:** after you intimidate a creature that was friendly towards you, it may lose that attitude towards you and your comrades. Intimidate a neutral creature, and it may become hostile.

**A note on deception:** if a creature finds out you deceived it, and does not consider your reason good enough — it becomes hostile to you.

### Advice for roleplay

Some important things to remember:

1. **Don't overdo it.** If you tend to roleplay a lot, check with the other players that you are not going overboard and slowing the game down. Roleplay is a core part of the game — but the most important thing is that the whole group is having fun.
2. **Check boundaries.** Some players are uncomfortable with certain topics and descriptions. Before the game starts, confirm with everyone what they are uncomfortable with, and what should not be brought up in play.
3. **Don't carry conflict out of the game.** Never bully another player or act hostile towards them over something that happened in game — talk it out and reach a peaceful resolution outside the game.
4. **Discuss dark concepts first.** No one should play an evil, overly chaotic or malicious character without discussing it with the rest of the group beforehand.

### Out of combat rolls

When you are neither in combat nor in a social interaction, you are in what's called the **intermediate phase**. This is where you pick locks in the dead of night, gather information by listening in, collect herbs in the wild, find your way through a maze, lift a rock to clear a path — and much more.

When you declare an action, the Qg decides which attribute you roll, according to the situation. Then, after you know the attribute but **before you roll**, you may decide how much time you dedicate to the task.

Each task has a **focus** value: the amount of time it takes to reduce the test score by 1. By focusing, you can reduce a test score to a minimum of **half its original value, rounded up**.

Common activities and their focus:

| Activity | Check | Focus |
|---|---|---|
| Lock picking | sense | 1 minute |
| Searching for knowledge | intellect | 2 hours |
| Gathering gossip | charisma | 1 hour |
| Sneaking through a guarded area | sense | 10 minutes |
| Climbing a high area | power | 5 minutes |
| Acrobatic maneuvers | speed | 30 seconds |
| Pickpocketing | speed | 2 minutes |
| Finding a path | intellect | 30 minutes |
| Creating traps | intellect | 15 minutes |
| Purging a poison from your body | endurance | 1 hour |

## Combat

This chapter will cover:

- How combat works: start and end
- In a turn: what are your options?
- Attacks: damage and additional effects
- Death (and other negative conditions)
- Ending combat: when the dust settles

### How combat works

**When does it start?**

Combat only really starts when one or more sides in a scene are clearly trying to accomplish something the other side is opposed to — and both are ready to use force to defend their position. In simple terms: only when both sides are aware of each other and hostile to one another.

- *Important: being aware of an enemy (say, hearing footsteps in the dark) does not mean you know exactly where they are.*

**What happens once it starts?**

The base initiative system of Rise of Mortals works in alternating slots: **hero → enemy → hero → enemy**, and so on around the table.

- **The first turn:** by default, the heroes act first. If the enemies set up an ambush or hold a clear situational advantage, the Qg may hand the first slot to them instead.
- **The hero slot:** the players decide together which hero acts in the current hero slot. You are free to change this internal order every round, if it benefits the group tactically.
- **The enemy slot:** the Qg — or the special rules of specific monsters — decides which enemy acts.
- **One important restriction:** the same hero cannot take two hero slots in a row, unless a special ability or effect explicitly allows it.
- **Enemies follow the same rules** in an ordinary fight — one slot at a time. Unlike heroes, enemies **cannot change their internal order**: some abilities depend on which combatants have not yet acted this round. If many enemies of the same type share a battlefield, they may be split into groups that act in different enemy slots.
- **Powerful creatures play by their own rules.** Some take two enemy slots with a pool of 4 moves per round, and true villains may act after every single hero. A creature's own entry defines this (see: the Game Master's handbook).

**Alternative rule — individual initiative:** if you prefer the classic, epic-fantasy approach, you can use an individual turn order instead. Turn order is determined by the total number of **sense and speed dice**: the more dice a character has, the earlier they act. All other combat mechanics stay unchanged.

### In a turn

**What is a turn?**

A creature's turn is a short span of time within a combat round. Each round lasts around 15 seconds, and a single turn takes only a fraction of that. Within the world of the game, all turns happen at nearly the same time, and their effects resolve within fractions of a second of one another. Enemies of the same type (such as a group of minions) can act together in one turn, to keep the fight simple and fast.

**What can I do on my turn?**

Each round, a character can perform up to **3 moves** — that is what a character's actions in combat are called, and most of them can only be taken on your turn. When you perform a move, announce it **before** rolling. You may perform the same move more than once (in practice, twice tends to be the optimum).

- You cannot perform the same **hostile** move more than once each turn.

### The move list

**Help another creature:** you spend your move preparing another character for success. Describe how you help — and when the character you helped makes their move, you add the same modifier they use. For example, if you help a character move a boulder, they add your power dice to their roll.

**Stride:** you move up to your movement value, in meters.

**Jump:** you move a number of meters equal to your power dice size, in a straight line — and you start falling if the move doesn't end on a solid surface.

- If you stride before jumping, the distance is doubled.
- You may trade distance for height: 4 meters of jump distance move you 1 meter upwards (so a 4-meter jump can carry you 1 meter up instead of 4 meters forward).
- For every die you have in power, your maximum jump distance (up or forward) increases by 1.
- You cannot jump while you have 0 movement.

**Hostile move:** a move meant to damage or impair a character (covered in full detail below).

**Guard:** this move can be taken when an attack hits you or when you take damage. You gain **−1 damage reduction** until the end of the current turn, and you ignore all additional effects of the attack. This stacks on top of your usual damage reduction — you are spending a resource for extra protection in the moment.

**Start casting a spell:** can only be taken by a character with at least one talent from the Mana manipulation talent tree. You begin changing the battlefield with mana.

**Finish / progress casting a spell:** can only be taken if you started casting a spell this turn or last turn, and if you performed this move this turn or last turn.

- Spells that require 1 move to finish take effect the moment you start casting them, and do not require this move.

**Change the scene:** you attempt something that advances your goal or helps conclude the battle — opening a river dam to stop a fire, boarding up windows so the enemies can't shoot through, collapsing a tunnel to escape the monsters.

**Intercept (costs 2 moves):** you try to intercept another character's move. Declare what you intend to intercept **before** you roll, then roll the appropriate attribute against the move: blocking a giant's strike is a power roll, deflecting an arrow is a speed check. If the move you are intercepting doesn't require a roll — striding, drinking a potion — the creature rolls an opposing check, using an attribute of the Qg's choice.

- Common interception attempts: *"When that creature moves, I trip them"* (power against the creature's speed or endurance); *"When I am attacked, I parry"* (speed against the attack roll); *"When the creature drinks the potion, I shatter the bottle"* (speed or power against the creature's speed or sense).
- You cannot make an attack roll with this move.

**Evade (costs 2 moves):** you gain **+1 evasion** until the start of your next turn. You cannot evade while restrained or while you have 0 movement.

**Whirlwind (costs 3 moves):** a special type of hostile move. You make an attack against **all** creatures within your reach — allies included. You roll only once, and apply that roll against the evasion of every creature within reach.

- To be clear: this move cannot be chosen when you are granted a free hostile move.

**Minor moves:** characters interact with plenty of things during their turn. Minor things — opening an unlocked door, closing a window, getting something out of your backpack — don't cost a whole move. We suggest allowing 2 or 3 of these per turn without charging a move for them.

**Free moves:** some talent trees and special traits grant a character a "free move". Free moves, hostile or not, do not count against your maximum of 3 — and if you make a hostile move as a free move, you can still make that same hostile move with one of your 3 regular moves this round.

### Movement rules

**Basic movement.** Every character has a basic movement speed — a walking speed. It represents the distance a character covers at a casual pace in about 15 seconds. If you only have your basic movement and want to climb, swim, or use another form of movement, you may spend one move and roll a **power or endurance check against a test score of 1**. On a success, you use that form of movement at half speed — rounded **down**.

#### Flying

Flying is a special type of movement, available only through magic or to creatures with natural wings (or another innate ability to fly). You cannot gain access to flying through the regular movement checks above.

When flying, you track one additional stat: **altitude**. Your default altitude is **0** — standing on the ground. Flying and gliding creatures gain access to the following actions:

**Fly up:** if you can fly, for every **5 meters** you move horizontally, you may increase your altitude by up to **2**. For every 1 point of altitude, you are 1 meter above the ground below you. Altitude is always relative to the ground below you — if you fly 10 meters and gain 6 altitude, but finish your turn above a space that is 4 meters high, you only have 2 altitude.

**Glide:** instead of *stride*, a character with the ability to fly or glide gains the glide action. The character moves horizontally, but can also reduce their altitude, gaining **1 extra movement for every point of altitude lowered**.

**Dive:** instead of gradually descending, you can plummet in a straight line downwards, gaining **+10 movement** this turn — but every meter of that bonus must be spent moving **straight down**. A dive is a controlled fall: if you hit the ground while diving, you take fall damage as if you had fallen for 1 turn — unless you pull up before the ground, or a feature lets you land safely.

Two rules bind all of the above:

- You cannot gain movement from lowering your altitude and raise your altitude on the same turn, unless a feature explicitly allows it.
- Your altitude cannot fall below 0 — unless you are ethereal and can pass through the floor.

#### Climbing

Climbing lets you move up walls and along ceilings. You can move up to **half your movement speed** when striding to climb, and you cannot stride more than once per turn while climbing. When you start moving, you must succeed on a **power check against a test score of 1** — on a failure, the move is wasted and you stay where you are.

The surface you climb can make things worse:

| Type of terrain | Effect |
|---|---|
| Hanging from a ceiling | you can only move up to 5 meters |
| Flat wall | the test score is increased by 2 |
| Jagged wall | on a failed climbing check, the climber falls off the wall |
| Spiky surface | climbers take 1 damage when they start moving |

#### Harsh terrain

Sometimes fighters find themselves on ground that is not comfortable to fight on — this is called harsh terrain. While standing on harsh terrain, a character **cannot evade**. Crossing harsh terrain costs **twice the movement**: a 7-meter patch of harsh terrain takes 14 meters of movement to cross.

#### Dangerous terrain

Extremely rare — and formidable: ground that damages the creatures crossing it. Harsh terrain that also counts as dangerous deals **1 damage** to anyone who spends movement to cross it. This damage can carry traits and damage tags — fire for a flaming field, bleed for a hallway with a spiked floor. In extreme cases the damage may be higher (such as lava dealing 10 damage, ignoring unphased).

#### Fall damage

Sometimes climbing doesn't go as planned — and you fall.

- When falling, you descend **your size × 5 meters** at the end of each turn.
- When you hit the ground, you take **damage equal to your size for every consecutive turn** you spent falling.

The bigger they are, the harder they fall — literally. Toppling a giant off a cliff is hard work (remember the size rules), but the reward matches the effort.

### Hostile moves

A hostile move is made when a character tries to hurt or impair another character. When you choose to make a hostile move — on your turn, or as part of an interception — you must choose: do you mean to **impair** the enemy, or to **damage** them?

#### Impair

Impairing a creature is a hostile move focused on reducing an enemy's effectiveness and giving your allies an advantage.

To impair a creature, first select the condition you intend to inflict, then use a move to try to inflict it. The check or move required is written in the condition itself.

**Ending an impairment.** On their turn, an impaired creature can either spend their movement or make a *change the scene* move to try to end the impairment. Another creature can also *change the scene* to try to end an impairment on someone else. How exactly a condition clears depends on the condition — being knocked prone, for example, is cleared by simply standing up with your movement, no check needed. Creatures can work together to remove a basic condition, following the same rules as the *help another creature* move.

**Can I impair multiple creatures with one move?**

No — unless a specific talent lets you.

**Can I be affected by two impairing effects of the same kind?**

No. If one creature restrains you by grappling, and then a second creature restrains you too, the effects do not stack — you still lower your evasion by 1 and lose only 1 success on power and speed rolls. But the second effect is not wasted: if the first one ends, the second takes its place, and you remain restrained. A character can try to remove several same-type effects at once by changing the scene — the test score is **1 + the number of effects** of that type they are trying to remove.

**What if multiple impairments affect the same attribute?**

They stack across *different* conditions. If you are restrained and prone, you reduce 2 successes from hostile rolls that use power or speed — restrained impairs power and speed, and prone impairs hostile moves.

#### Basic negative conditions

##### Restrained

A restrained creature cannot move because of an external force — another creature grappling it, or the environment itself.

- **Inflict:** make a power check against the target's power or speed check. On a success, the creature is restrained. This condition can also be applied by spells, items and other effects.
- **Effect:** the creature gets −1 success on power and speed checks, and its evasion is reduced by 1. The creature cannot leave the spot it was restrained in or spend movement — but a grappler can still pull it along when they move.
- **Clear:** make a contested power check against the test score of the restraining effect (1, unless specified otherwise). On a success, the creature is freed.
- *Note: if you are grappled, the test score to escape equals the number of successes the grappler rolled.*

##### Prone

A prone creature is lying on the ground — or falling through the air with no ability to fly.

- **Inflict:** make a power, speed or intellect check (the way you try to knock the target down determines the attribute) against the target's **size or evasion, whichever is higher**. On a success, the target falls prone. You cannot knock down a creature two or more sizes bigger than you.
- **Effect:** the creature loses 1 success from hostile attack rolls, and gets −1 evasion against attacks originating within 5 meters of it. While prone, the creature can only move a number of meters equal to its size.
- **Clear:** if nothing else stops you from moving (a grappled creature, for example, cannot), simply spend 10 meters of movement and stand up.

##### Intimidated

An intimidated creature has seen or heard something that shakes it with terror — a clear threat, or the roar of a dragon.

- **Inflict:** some abilities cause this condition, but anyone can attempt it by making a charisma check against the target's willpower check. If you have more power dice than the target, you gain +1 success on the roll. On a success, the target is intimidated.
- **Effect:** the creature gets −1 success on all intellect, willpower and charisma checks — and −1 on hostile rolls against the creature who intimidated it.
- **Clear:** the condition lasts a number of rounds equal to the successes rolled by whoever intimidated you (the very test score you failed to beat). You can simply wait it out — or spend a move to fight it: roll a check, and every success shaves one round off the counter. It is a willpower check when clearing the condition from yourself, and a charisma check when clearing it from another creature.
- *Example: you are intimidated for 3 rounds. You spend a move, roll your willpower and get 1 success — now you only wait 2 rounds.*

##### Taunted

A taunted creature is fixated on harming one creature, mostly ignoring everyone else.

- **Inflict:** besides talents and abilities that inflict this condition, a creature can taunt another by making a charisma check against the target's intellect or willpower check. On a success, the target is taunted by you until the start of your next turn.
- **Effect:** the creature gets −2 successes on hostile moves against anyone who did not taunt it, and −1 on sense checks against creatures that did not taunt it. If it moves, it must move towards the creature that taunted it.
- **Clear:** a creature may use a *change the scene* move to remove the condition from itself or from a creature next to it.
- *Note: a creature can only be taunted by one creature at a time.*

##### Blinded

A blinded creature simply cannot use its eyesight — a burst of bright light, total darkness, sand in the eyes.

- **Inflict:** make a speed or intellect check against the target's endurance or speed. On a success, the target is blinded. Some talents and features also inflict this condition.
- **Effect:** the creature loses 1 success from sense checks and attack rolls, cannot use abilities that require line of sight, and its base evasion is reduced to 1.
- **Clear:** blinded is a special condition — it clears automatically at the end of the affected creature's turn.

#### Unique negative conditions

##### Unaware

An unaware creature does not know the position of a threat or enemy — even if it suspects something is there.

- **Inflict:** this condition is applied automatically when a creature does not know your location and presence. You can use a stealth check to hide (see: [Stealth and hiding, later in this chapter](#stealth-and-hiding)), giving the unaware condition to every creature that failed to notice you. This condition counts for a *brutal* weapon — unless the creature is aware of you specifically.
- **Effect:** when you attack a creature that is unaware of you specifically, its evasion is reduced by 2. An unaware creature also cannot guard.
- **Clear:** when a creature stops being hidden, every instance of unaware it inflicted ends (see: [Stealth and hiding](#stealth-and-hiding)).

##### Weakened

A weakened creature has its strength sapped — by poison, exhaustion, or magical draining.

- **Inflict:** weakened is special: most of the time, the target must beat a **test score** written on the ability that inflicts the condition, instead of making a contested check against the initiator. You are also automatically weakened when your **mana reaches 0**.
- **Effect:** the creature loses 1 success from any endurance, speed and power check, and its movement is halved (rounded down).
- **Clear:** the way you gained this condition dictates how it can be cleared.

##### Poisoned

A poisoned creature is afflicted by a harmful agent — toxic ooze, poisonous air, a laced blade.

- **Inflict:** there is no check to inflict poison organically — you need a potion or a special ability. The three common delivery methods:
  - **Lacing:** applying poison to a bladed weapon, a thrown weapon, a bow or a crossbow costs 1 move. The poison lasts 1 minute, or until you hit an attack with the laced weapon.
  - **Injecting:** *change the scene* and make a speed check against the target's evasion. If the target is unaware of you, or doesn't realize your hostile intent, it costs a move with no check.
  - **Ingesting:** the creature must eat food or drink a liquid infused with the poison, and swallow.
- **Effect:** a poisoned creature cannot guard, and striding costs it 1 extra move. In addition, a poison may target an attribute, giving −1 success with it — this differs from poison to poison.
- **Clear:** use a *change the scene* move to make an endurance check against the poison's original test score — on a success, the effect ends. If no test score is mentioned, the poison cannot be removed early. Some poisons wear off after a set time, written in the poison's description; some can only be resisted at intervals (a forest wyvern's poison, for example, allows a *change the scene* attempt only once every 2 hours).

#### Attack

The second kind of hostile move is an attack. When a creature attacks, it makes a **power or speed check against the enemy's evasion** as the test score. If you roll equal to or more than the enemy's evasion, the attack hits — and the target takes **damage equal to the number of successes** of the attack.

- *A note on damage reduction:* damage reduction can reduce damage all the way to 0.
- *Attacks made with speed:* speed attacks never deal more than **1 point of base damage**, no matter how many successes were rolled. Some items and abilities ignore this rule, but they must say so explicitly. Extra damage — from talents, spells and the like — is still added on top.
- *Attacks that use neither power nor speed* (most of them spell-related) deal full damage, unless stated otherwise.

#### Fighting techniques

When you roll an attack, you can enhance it with a fighting technique. Techniques add effects to your attack — but raise the target's evasion, as you aim for a more precise spot on the body. (Some creatures are immune to the effects of debilitating strikes — a slime, notably, lacks both organs and limbs.) A fighting technique can be used **only once each round**.

**Technique ranks.** There are three ranks, and every technique belongs to one. The higher the rank, the stronger the technique — and when you use one, **its rank is added to the enemy's evasion** for that attack. A rank 2 technique raises the target's evasion by 2.

**Learning techniques:**

- When you complete a combat-related talent tree, a fighting technique is one of the rewards you can pick (see: [The talent system — talent point upgrades](#talent-point-upgrades)).
- When the combined rank of all techniques you know reaches **5, or any multiple of 5** (10, 15, 20...), you learn one **rank 3** technique.
- Temporarily learned techniques (such as from the harsh training activity) do not count towards that total — and neither do techniques granted by talents.

##### Rank 1 techniques

**Focused aim** *(ranged or deadly weapon)*: the attack gains +1d6 to the roll.

**Heavy strike** *(two-handed melee weapon)*: on a hit, you deal 2 extra damage.

**Draw blood** *(any bladed weapon)*: the attack inflicts 3 ranks of bleed.

**Feint** *(any melee weapon)*: on a hit, the target gets −1 on attack rolls against you until the start of your next turn.

**Bind** *(any melee weapon)*: on a hit, you enter a bind with your opponent. Neither of you can use your weapons to attack other creatures while within each other's reach — until the end of your next turn, or until your opponent *changes the scene* to exit the bind.

**Dazing strike** *(any weapon)*: on a hit, the creature cannot intercept other creatures until the start of the next round.

**Counter strike** *(requires intercepting an attack)*: if you successfully intercept a creature's attack, you may immediately strike the attacker with the same weapon you intercepted with — if they are within your reach.

**Positional maneuver** *(any weapon)*: before or after the attack, you may switch positions with an adjacent creature — you take their space, they take yours. If the switch would make any creature overlap with another creature or an object, the technique cannot be used.

##### Rank 2 techniques

**Elegant strike** *(speed attack)*: on a hit, you gain +2 evasion against the next attack that targets you.

**Unbreakable strike** *(power attack)*: the attack gains *Armor breaker*. If it already has *Armor breaker*, it gains *Armor destroyer*. If it already has *Armor destroyer*, it deals 3 extra damage.

**Trip attack** *(any melee weapon)*: on a hit, the target falls prone.

**Taunting attack** *(any melee weapon)*: on a hit, the creature is taunted by you until the start of your next turn.

**Intimidating strike** *(any melee weapon)*: on a hit, the creature must succeed on a willpower resist roll — the test score is the damage the attack dealt. On a failure, it is intimidated.

**Disarm** *(any non-brutal weapon)*: on a hit, the creature drops one weapon or object it is holding. A fragile object may break when dropped (roll 1d8 — on a success, it breaks).

**Diversion** *(any weapon)*: on a hit, the target's evasion is reduced by 3 until it takes damage.

**Blinding strike** *(any weapon)*: on a hit, the target is blinded.

**Bypass** *(any weapon)*: this attack ignores the evasion bonus of a raised shield, and cannot be redirected to it.

**Advancing strike** *(any weapon)*: on a hit, you or an ally move up to 15 meters. On a miss, your ally still moves 5 meters.

**Titanic blow** *(any weapon)*: on a hit, the creature is pushed back 15 meters.

**Mage disrupter** *(any weapon)*: on a hit, if the creature is in the middle of casting a spell, it immediately stops casting.

**Battle cry** *(any melee weapon)*: when you use this technique, all enemies within your reach become intimidated (test score of 1 to remove).

##### Rank 3 techniques

**Killing intent** *(any weapon)*: the attack deals 5 extra damage if it lands. If it misses, the target is intimidated by you (test score of 2 to remove).

**Rally** *(any weapon)*: after you hit, one creature that can hear you clears a negative condition affecting it. You cannot rally yourself.

**Combo strike** *(any weapon)*: if you hit, an ally who can attack your target may immediately take a hostile move against it (this still costs the ally 1 move).

**Grapple strike** *(any one-handed melee weapon)*: after you hit a creature, you may immediately try to grapple it. You count as rolling a success on every die used to restrain — but you still follow the regular rules of grappling.

**Savage brawl** *(any weapon)*: you make **two attacks** instead of one. Both are affected by the evasion increase, and both must target different creatures.

**Manaheart** *(any weapon)*: right after the attack, you immediately cast a spell, changing its casting time to 1 move — and paying extra mana for every move the spell normally costs to cast, based on its level: beginner +1 mana per move, advanced +2, master +3, legendary +4.

### Damage, resilience and unphased

**What is damage?**

Damage represents the harm done to creatures in a dangerous situation. The simplest example: a character is hit by an attack with 3 successes and has no damage reduction — they take 3 damage.

Damage represents every kind of injury, so there are no separate damage types in Rise of Mortals — instead, attacks with certain keywords carry additional effects. A lightning bolt and a sword swing do not hurt the same way: the bolt has the *electric* damage tag, the sword has the *bladed* weapon trait.

Damage only represents lethal harm — a cat scratching your leg while playing does not count as the creature dealing damage.

**What is unphased?**

A character's unphased is a buffer of damage they can absorb while still operating at full capacity. Damage always reduces unphased first; only once unphased reaches 0 (or if an effect specifically ignores unphased) does damage reduce resilience.

**What is resilience?**

A character's resilience is the amount of damage they can take once their unphased runs out. When resilience reaches 0, the character becomes Ashen (see: [Death, and the path of ash](#death-and-the-path-of-ash)).

Damage is **cumulative** for both unphased and resilience over the course of a fight: if a creature with 2 unphased takes 1 damage and then 2 damage, the third point spills over — 1 damage to its resilience.

**What is heavily wounded?**

Heavily wounded is a condition a creature gains once both of the following are true:

- The creature has 0 unphased.
- The creature's current resilience is lower than its maximum.

In other words, you become heavily wounded the moment damage bites into your resilience — not the moment your unphased runs out. A creature at exactly 0 unphased with full resilience is battered, but not yet heavily wounded.

*Example: a character with 3 unphased and 4 resilience is fine for the first 3 points of damage (their unphased drops to 0). The 4th point of damage reduces their resilience to 3/4 and they become heavily wounded. At 7 total damage their resilience reaches 0 — and they become Ashen.*

A heavily wounded creature gets **−1 evasion** and can make **1 less move** each round. In addition, some attacks and talents can only target heavily wounded creatures.

### Damage tags

Damage tags give an attack special properties — but usually do not add extra damage to the strike itself.

**Fire:** the attack is made of fire, or fire is a big part of it. Fire attacks **ignore 1 point of damage reduction** and apply **1 continual fire damage** — but deal half damage (rounded down) if the target is covered in water.

**Electric:** the attack is made with lightning or carries an electrical current. Electric attacks **shock** the target: a shocked creature loses 1 success on all rolls on its next turn. Underwater, electric attacks deal **double damage** and shock every creature within 3 meters of the original target.

**Draining:** the attack feeds its user. If it deals damage to a **heavily wounded** creature, the attacker regains 1 resilience.

**Mana drain:** the target reduces the damage from their **mana** instead, and takes no damage from the attack (unless specified otherwise). The attack ignores damage reduction. After the attack resolves, the attacker regains half the mana drained this way (rounded up). If an AoE effect carries mana drain, the user only gains 1 mana per target.

**Frost:** the attack chills the target: its movement is **halved** until the end of combat, or until it *changes the scene* to remove every frost effect currently on it. Frost effects stack, and each new one halves the target's **current** movement — a creature with 20 meters drops to 10 after the first frost effect, 5 after the second — but frost can never reduce movement below **5 meters**. Underwater, a frost attack also **restrains** the target until it succeeds on a power check against a test score of 1.

- Fire thaws frost: a creature that takes fire damage regains its normal movement (and stops being restrained, if it was frozen underwater). Likewise, taking frost damage removes all continual fire damage from the creature.

**Condition inflicting:** the condition's name is written on the attack (a poisoned dagger inflicts *poisoned*). On a hit, the target is impaired by that condition until the end of its next turn.

**Continual damage:** after being hit by an attack with this tag, the creature takes damage at the end of **every round**, equal to the tag's rank. If the original attack carried a special tag like fire, the continual damage carries it too — which means continual **fire** damage grows its own counter by 1 every time it deals damage. Continual damage ignores damage reduction unless specified otherwise. The affected creature can end the effect by *changing the scene* — no check required, it only costs the move.

**Bleed:** a special kind of continual damage. The number of bleed ranks inflicted equals the damage the original attack dealt. At the end of each round, the creature takes damage equal to **half its bleed ranks (rounded up)** — and its bleed rank then drops by 3. Bleed damage does not inherit any tags from the original attack.

- A bleeding creature can spend a move to *change the scene* and **hold the bleed**: it takes no damage that round, but the bleed rank only drops by 2 instead of 3.

### Changing the scene

When a character wants to use their equipment in a non-hostile way, remove a negative condition, or do anything that isn't covered by another move — they default to *change the scene*. Throwing a rope, forcing a locked door, or any other action that changes the battlefield in any way: you describe what you want to do, and the Qg asks for an appropriate roll.

Talents are rarely added to these rolls, but you can always ask the Qg to add the bonus of any of them — when it makes sense thematically, such as using *flash step* to reach a spot before someone else, or *power strike* + *brute force* to bust open a door.

Changing the scene also covers changing the status of the characters themselves: rolling to hide, taking cover, removing an impairment through a check, switching weapons — and even special types of movement like climbing, swimming or jumping.

### Extra rules for combat

#### Visibility and cover

When you measure line of sight, things get in the way — smoke, walls, bushes. A character positioned behind one of them gains a form of cover:

**Solid cover:** when a character is attacked by a ranged weapon and the line of sight is at least partly obscured by an object or a piece of hard terrain, the target gains **+1 evasion**, as more than half their body is covered. Cover also grants **−2 damage reduction against area effects** that spread around corners (AoE damage does *not* ignore the damage reduction granted by cover).

- A character can *change the scene* to gain solid cover from smaller objects — any shield other than a buckler, a small cabinet. The object must be at least a quarter of the character's size.

**Soft cover:** when a character is obscured by thick brush, a thin wall or another creature, they can *take cover* to gain the benefits of solid cover — but they get no passive benefit.

**Abstract cover:** when you cannot see a creature because of a non-solid effect — smoke, mist, heavy rain, darkness and the like (called an *obscuring sensory effect*) — you cannot make hostile moves against it until you succeed on a **sense check to pinpoint its location**. The test score is 1 — but if the creature hid by *changing the scene*, the test score is the number of successes it rolled (even if that's lower). You cannot *take cover* while benefiting only from abstract cover. A crowd of 3 or more people also counts as abstract cover — and when **5 or more creatures** collapse into a disorderly melee, the brawl itself becomes an obscuring effect: to target your enemy inside it, you must first **pinpoint** them, like with any abstract cover.

**Complete cover:** a character fully obscured by a solid object (such as a wall) cannot be targeted directly by any ability.

**Exiting cover:** after taking cover, you stay in cover until you move from that spot — willingly or not — or until your cover is destroyed.

#### Stealth and hiding

When rolling for stealth, you must be **out of sight** of the creature you are hiding from. Stealth is generally rolled with **sense** — in rare cases, the Qg may ask for a different attribute.

The test score is 1, unless the creatures you are hiding from are actively looking for you, or have special abilities that make stealth harder. **The number of successes you roll becomes the test score** for any creature trying to perceive you or sense your location.

You stay hidden for as long as you want — or until one of the following happens:

1. You are seen clearly by another creature — in bright light, or while the creature is within 5 meters of you.
2. You move more meters in one turn than your **sense dice size × sense dice number**.
3. You make a very loud noise while a creature is within hearing distance.
4. A creature passes the test score to pinpoint your location.
5. You willingly stop hiding.

Additional rules:

- You can try to hide with only abstract cover (no solid or soft cover) — at **−2 successes** to your roll.
- Even while you are hidden, creatures can use clues and abilities to discover your location — and every time you make a **hostile move or cast a spell** while hidden, the test score to discover you drops by 2. If that test score ever reaches 0 or below, you are immediately discovered.
- If you try to hide again on the same turn you were revealed, you get **−1 success for each time** you successfully re-hid this way during the encounter.

#### Temporary increases

When an effect grants you temporary unphased or resilience:

- It disappears after **1 minute**.
- Temporary points are always lost **before** your permanent unphased or resilience.
- Temporary points cannot be regained by healing.
- If several sources grant you temporary increases, only **one** of them applies.

#### Area of effect

Attacks that target spaces rather than a specific creature (AoE for short) work a little differently:

- Damage is not rolled by the attacker. Instead, every creature in the area makes a **check**, and each success reduces the damage taken by 1. Some effects — certain spells among them — allow no roll at all, dealing full damage.
- AoE attacks **ignore damage reduction**, unless stated otherwise. The one exception: damage reduction granted by **cover** still applies (see: [Visibility and cover](#visibility-and-cover)).
- If an AoE completely covers an **object** (not a creature), it deals double damage to it.

#### Killing blow

When a creature is completely at your mercy — asleep, bound and helpless, unaware with a blade already at its throat — you may simply kill it. No attack roll, no damage counting. A killing blow is a narrative tool rather than a combat move: the Qg decides when a target truly qualifies, and enemies can use it just as well as heroes can. (This is why you set a watch.)

### Death, and the path of ash

**What happens when you fall to 0 resilience?**

When an ordinary enemy falls to 0 resilience, it simply dies — a guard or a goblin crumbles under wounds that severe. Heroes, villains and some ancient monsters are different: their will carries them further. When such a character falls to 0 resilience, they enter a state called **Ashen**.

While Ashen, a character:

- can only make **1 move** each round;
- is **immune to damage** — but not to negative effects;
- must make a **cinder check** every time they *would* take damage: a willpower check against a test score equal to the number of rounds they have been Ashen. On a failure, they die on the spot.

**Leaving the ash.** At the start of their turn, an Ashen character may choose to exit the state, regaining 1 resilience. But the path back has a price — and it steepens:

- The **first** time a character exits the Ashen state, they become weakened. This weakened condition can only be removed by 6 or more hours of sleep.
- The **second** time, they are knocked out for 1 minute.
- The **third** time — they die.

Both counters — the rounds spent Ashen (which feed the cinder check) and the number of times you exited the state — only reset after **6 or more hours of sleep**. They persist between fights: a hero who spent 4 rounds Ashen in the morning starts their next cinder check that evening against a test score of 4. Sleep is an action you take in a safe place — use it. Or, the best advice this book can offer: try not to die twice in one day.

**Healing an Ashen character.** An Ashen character can be healed — but the moment they regain resilience, they exit the Ashen state, **and it counts towards the exit counter**. Abilities that restore unphased or resilience automatically at any point of a round are disabled while their owner is Ashen.

A character can stay Ashen for a maximum of **1 hour**. After that, they die.

### Ending combat

**How does combat end?**

1. All enemies surrender, run away or die.
2. The heroes reach their goal — the prisoners they came for are free, the artifact is out of the ancient wizard's tomb.
3. All heroes fall unconscious, escape or surrender.

**When combat ends:**

- All heroes regain all their unphased (but not resilience).
- Effects with a *once per combat* tag that last any amount of time beyond an instant — end.

### Optional rule: Grievous injury

When a heavily wounded hero takes damage, the wound may leave a mark:

| Damage taken | Effect |
|---|---|
| 1 | nothing |
| 2–3 | the character gains 3 ranks of bleed |
| 4–7 | internal injury: the character can only make 1 move on their turn this round — and if they already made at least one, they cannot make any more moves until the start of the next round (free moves included) |
| 8+ | roll a d6: the character loses an arm (1), a leg (2), an eye (3) — or nothing (4–6) |

The lasting consequences:

- A character who loses a **leg** cannot stride more than once each turn, and their movement is reduced by 5 meters. Losing both legs leaves the character permanently prone.
- A character who loses an **arm** cannot hold two objects at once, or a two-handed object. Losing both hands means no weapons other than unarmed strikes — and no holding objects.
- A character who loses both **eyes** is permanently blinded.

This rule is meant for extremely brutal games only, and should be discussed with the players before being implemented.

## Traveling: The Part Most of You Will Skip

This chapter will cover:

- Travel pace: the part most of you will probably read just to say how many days the trip takes
- Traveling activities: what can I do while walking?
- Traveling options: do I *have* to walk there?
- Environment types: when the ground is hostile
- Camping and resting: how to recover resources
- Full rest and safe havens: types, activities, locations and variants

### Travel pace

Travel pace is determined by a character's **movement speed and endurance** — and the group always travels at the rate of its slowest member.

**How long can you travel?** Your endurance decides: for every die you have in endurance, you can travel **1 hour at top speed** — or **2 hours per die, if your endurance die is a d8 or above**. Moving at half your top speed **doubles** the time you can travel.

If you travel beyond your travel value before getting at least 6 hours of rest, you become **weakened** until you rest for a total of 10 hours.

**How fast do you travel?** Divide your movement speed by 2.5 (or divide it by 5, then double the result) — that is how many kilometers you cover per hour.

A character's **travel value** is written as **X;Y** — X kilometers per hour, for Y hours.

*Example: Ben (movement 18, endurance 1d8) has a travel value of 7.2;2 — a hard march of 7.2 km/h for 2 hours. At half speed, that becomes 3.6;4. Fractions are normal here: the world map has no grid.*

### Traveling activities: what can I do while walking?

While traversing the lands of Rise of Mortals, you can do more than just walk. Only characters moving at **half their speed** can use a traveling activity — so if a character who covers 10 km/h at half speed travels with one who covers 15 km/h at full speed, the group moves at 10 km/h, but only the first character gets an activity. You cannot switch activities mid-travel.
