**Scout ahead:** for the duration of this traveling activity, you gain knowledge of the terrain and its surroundings, including any notable features of the land or unique structures — seeing up to 2 km in a radius around the traveling group. A traveling group with at least 1 character scouting ahead gains **+1 success** to rolls in the first round of combat, if they ambush another creature.

**Covering tracks:** any creature trying to follow the party gets **−1 success** on rolls made to track it. The penalty grows by 1 for every additional character covering tracks.

**Keep an eye out:** you are ready for danger, and prepared to act. You roll an additional **1d8** on all sense checks to discover hidden details or creatures.

**Laying low:** you keep the whole group in check, instructing everyone how to travel quietly and swiftly. The entire traveling group gains **+1 success** on any roll made to hide or stay unnoticed. The bonus grows by 1 for every additional character using this activity.

**Tracking:** you focus on following another creature's trail. You can only take this activity with a specific target in mind — you are looking for particular clues to deduce its path. Roll a **sense check**: the test score is normally 1, but rises if the target covers its tracks, and is 2 if you are tracking a **single** creature. For every 6 creatures in the group you are tracking, the test score drops by 2 — and groups of more than 12 cannot cover their tracks at all. On a success, you know the direction your target has gone, until your next rest.

**Camping on the go:** while other activities affect the travel itself, this one enhances your next rest. Choose one camp activity: if you use it on your next rest, you add **a quarter of the time you traveled** to the hours spent on that activity.

### Traveling options: do I have to walk there?

Most people travel by foot — but Drasil offers alternatives. Every means of travel has its own travel value, its upkeep, and its perks.

- *Note: you cannot scout ahead while using an alternative travel option, if the whole group uses it instead of walking.*

**Riding a mount:** you cannot cover your tracks. The travel value is the animal's own — and if the mount can fly or swim, you cannot be tracked by normal means, provided you fly or swim for the full travel day. Upkeep: extra food every day to keep the mount healthy. The inventory cost of everything you carry counts towards the animal's load.

**Riding a vehicle:** you cannot cover your tracks or lay low. The travel value depends on the vehicle, and you can travel a number of hours equal to its travel value — even beyond a full travel day. You only stop to refuel (if the vehicle needs fuel), and you rest the normal amount, unless you can rest while the vehicle moves.

#### Mounts

**Riding horse** — travel value: **12;5**. Upkeep: an average horse weighs 420 kg and eats 1 inventory point of food every 2 days. Carrying capacity: 7 inventory points.

**Giant eagle** — travel value: **40;6**. Altitude: can fly up to 2,500 meters, but mostly stays around 1,500. Upkeep: an average giant eagle weighs about 225 kg and needs 2 inventory points of food each day. Carrying capacity: 5 inventory points.

#### Vehicles

**Carriage** — requires a horse (or a similarly sized animal) to pull it. Travel value: the same as the animal's, but it moves at half speed. Upkeep: none of its own. Carrying capacity: 25 inventory points.

**White smoker** (a steam-powered car) — travels at **50 km per hour**, and needs 3 liters of water per 10 km to power the engine. The tank holds up to 90 liters. Fits creatures up to size 4, plus a trunk for anything under 1.75 meters — the trunk carries up to 6 inventory points.

**Sky surfer** (a magical zeppelin) — extremely rare, and requires hiring at least **4 mages** to hold it at a steady altitude at all times. Travels at **70 km per hour** and stays up for as long as the mages keep it there. Can fly up to 4,000 meters. Upkeep: extra food for every mage. Carrying capacity: 75 inventory points.

### Environment types: when the ground is hostile

Drasil has its peaceful lands — but those are few, and rarely need a hero's help. Instead, the group will have the pleasure of traveling through the world's many hostile environments.

#### Desert

A wasteland of sand and dune — brutal and unforgiving.

- Traveling for more than 12 hours without at least 6 hours of rest gives you the **weakened** condition.
- A hero unable to eat and drink properly every 12 hours becomes **weakened**.
- When a hero finds food through the *search for food* camp activity, the Qg rolls a d4: on a 4, everyone who eats it is **poisoned** for the next 24 hours — you prepared it wrong, or didn't know the ingredient was inedible.
- A hero wearing armor in the desert spends **1 extra inventory point** on food. If they don't rest every 12 hours, their maximum unphased drops by 1 until they rest for at least 8 hours.
- Weakened conditions gained in the desert can only be removed by a **full rest**.

#### Mountain

Treacherous terrain and high cliffs are all that await the brave souls crossing the mountains of Drasil.

- A group that does not scout ahead risks a dead end: the Qg rolls a d6 — on a success, the trail is clear; otherwise the group loses **4 hours** of travel time finding the right path.
- Traveling through the mountains at **full speed** is a gamble. The Qg rolls a d4:

| d4 | Event |
|---|---|
| 1 | **Landslide:** every character in the group makes a speed or endurance resist roll against 5 armor-breaking damage. |
| 2 | **Chasm:** one randomly picked traveler makes a speed or sense check (test score 1). On a failure, they fall into a chasm — taking falling damage equal to **5 times their size**. |
| 3 | **Rockfall:** the rocky terrain crumbles behind you — there is no traveling back through this mountain. |
| 4 | **Safe passage:** the travel goes as normal. |

#### Grasslands

Some think grasslands are peaceful — but the open field lets predators spot prey from miles away. You cannot **lay low** in a grassland while traveling during the day.

#### Forest

The trees and brush hide approaching threats: you cannot **keep an eye out** in a forest.

Travel without scouting ahead, and you might get lost. The Qg secretly rolls a d6 — on a success, you are lost, and the Qg rolls a d10 for your actual direction:

| d10 | Direction |
|---|---|
| 1 | north |
| 2 | north-west |
| 3 | west |
| 4 | south-west |
| 5 | south |
| 6 | south-east |
| 7 | east |
| 8 | north-east |
| 9–10 | the correct direction after all |

#### Coastal

Salt and spray are not kind to steel. Metal equipment must be maintained every 12 hours, or its quality suffers:

- A **weapon** left unpolished for 3 days permanently loses 1 quality.
- An **armor** left unpolished for 2 days permanently drops its mobility value by 1 stage — and if mobility is already at its lowest, its defense value drops by 1 instead.

#### Swamp

Murky waters and the roots of ancient trees cover the ground of Drasil's swamps.

- You cannot **scout ahead**, and your traveling speed is reduced by 1 km per hour.
- The test score to track other creatures in a swamp is increased by 1.
- When meditating, you regain only **half your mana** every 24 hours.

#### Sky-cloud

Above the clouds, your main concern is not the weather — it is the heavenly currents that move the clouds around the world. Every creature that stays above the clouds for 24 hours makes a **willpower check** (test score 1). On a failure, they **permanently lose 1 point of maximum unphased**.

If your maximum unphased reaches 0 this way, your character dies of heavenly visions.

#### Seafaring

Sailing the seas replaces the usual routines with special traveling activities:

- **Navigate:** make an intellect check (test score 1). On a success, you travel in the right direction for the next 48 hours.
- **Secure the sails:** if no one takes this activity, the ship travels at half speed.

You cannot track, scout ahead or cover your tracks while seafaring — but you *may* use camping activities aboard the ship while it travels.

#### Underwater

- You cannot use fire while traveling underwater outside a vehicle.
- Without access to clean air — or the ability to breathe water — you cannot travel underwater at all.
- Creatures that are not underwater themselves cannot track you by non-magical means.

#### Tundra

The cold is a merciless lord, its chilling influence encroaching on everything that lives under it.

- You cannot travel at **full speed** through snow, and you cannot **camp on the go**.
- When camping, one camper must either spend an inventory point on firewood, or roll a sense check and spend 2 hours gathering it (at least 1 success needed). Every repeated search within the same 20 km radius raises the test score by 1.
- A fire must be lit every **24 hours**. No fire — you become **weakened**. And if you fail to light a fire within 36 hours of becoming weakened *in any fashion*, you die in the cold.

#### Abhorrent

An abhorrent land carries a vile curse, corrupting those who cross it.

- Spend more than **48 hours** in an abhorrent land, and you become **weakened**. If a weakened creature stays another 24 hours, it dies — and rises undead.
- No food can be found here: the *search for food* activity is unavailable, and you spend **double** the inventory points on food.

### Camping and resting: how to recover resources

After a long day of traveling or a hard battle, the heroes will want to rest up for the next day.

**The cost of skipping rest:**

- A creature must take the *sleeping* activity for at least **6 hours** to stay in top condition. If you go without sleep for more days than you have **endurance dice**, you become **weakened** until you complete an 8-hour rest.
- If you don't eat for a number of days equal to **double your endurance dice** (triple, if your endurance die is a d12), you become weakened.
- If you carry a weakened condition from lack of sleep **and** one from lack of food at the same time, you are **knocked out** — and wake up only once one of the two is removed.

**How camping works.** When you pick a place to rest, declare **in advance** how many hours you intend to rest. During that time, you take camp activities:

- You can repeat the same activity, unless stated otherwise.
- You can switch activities at any time — but you only gain an activity's bonus if you did it for its **full duration**. Activities with no set time grant their benefit when you finish them.
- It is assumed you eat the necessary amount while doing other activities.

#### List of camp activities

**Sleeping** *(at least 6 hours)*: you must sleep to avoid the penalties above. For every 2 hours you sleep beyond the required 6, you gain an extra **1d4 on sense checks** for the next 24 hours. If a battle starts while you sleep, you are unconscious — and can be killed if a creature uses a killing blow on you.

**Search for food** *(at least 1 hour)*: roll a **sense check for every hour** spent. You find enough food to feed a total size equal to the successes you rolled overall — 5 successes over 3 hours feed up to a total of size 5.

**Maintain equipment** *(1 hour)*: choose one piece of equipment — it gains its **full quality bonus** for the next 24 hours. Every additional hour maintains one more piece.

**Keep watch** *(at least 1 hour)*: you gain **+1 success on all sense rolls** made during your watch — and if battle starts while you are on the post, you gain **+1 success on all your rolls in the first round** of that battle.

**Heal up** *(3 hours)*: choose one resting character — a sleeping one, or yourself. That character regains **1 resilience**. A character can benefit from this activity only once per 24 hours.

**Polish armor** *(2 hours)*: choose one armor worn by you or another resting character — it regains its **default mobility value** for the next 24 hours. Repeatable, a different armor each time.

**Repair shield** *(1 hour)*: choose one shield and restore it to **full resilience**. Repeatable, a different shield each time.

**Meditate** *(2 hours)*: you regain mana equal to your **mana regeneration** — 2 for every intellect die (4 per die, if your intellect die is a d8 or above), plus any bonuses to mana regeneration you've gained from talents.

**Training an attribute** *(2 hours)*: choose power, speed or endurance — you gain **+1d4 on all rolls** with that attribute for the next 24 hours. Repeatable, a different attribute each time.

**Craft an item** *(at least 1 hour)*: you spend rest time creating a new permanent item.

- Every inventory point the item takes costs **12 hours** of crafting. Double the total time for every quality level above *poor*.
- To craft anything better than *poor* quality, you need one of these talent trees: **Engineer, Forgemaster, Fixer, Alchemist or Squire**.
- With **10+ talent points**, you may craft *pristine* items. With **25+**, you may craft *mastercraft* items with 1 enchantment.
- You can stop crafting at any point (unless the item says otherwise) and pick the process back up later.
- For every hour of crafting **outside a safe haven**, roll an intellect check: the test score is 1 for *poor* items, +1 for every quality level above. On a failure, the item demands one more hour.

**Raise morale** *(1 hour)*: requires at least one other character, but can include as many as you want. Everyone who participates for the full duration gains **+1d10 on all checks against fear or taunt effects**.

### Full rest: when the adventure ends

When the heroes reach a **safe haven** and rest there for a full day or more, they gain a full rest: every hero regains **4 resilience and 4 mana**, the group regains its spent **inventory points** (see the haven types below), and non-permanent debuffs — curses, stat reductions — end. Arriving does *not* restore you fully: for that, spend your activity slots (*complete rest* exists for a reason).

Once a hero has spent all their activity slots in a safe haven, they gain **no further benefit** from staying — you've done everything this place has to offer, and the road calls.

- The inventory points refilled by resting are not individual — the **group as a whole** regains them, and decides who gets what (or uses the group inventory system).
- The food a location supplies represents its **surplus**: you can spend inventory points or money to get more, and a safe haven's supply resets every season.

### Safe haven types

| Type | Population | Inventory points regained | Food supplied | Activity slots per hero |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| **Hamlet** | under 100 | 2 | first day | 1 |
| **Village** | 250–300 | 5 | first 3 days | 2 |
| **Town** | a few thousand | 8 | first 7 days | 4 |
| **City** | up to 15,000+ | 11 | first 15 days | 7 |

**Hamlet:** the smallest settlement that still counts as a safe haven — a fortress, a small survivor camp, a bandit base, a fishing village. A hamlet cannot be a trade center.

**Village:** the most common safe haven in Drasil — small trade posts, rural farmland, large tribal camps.

**Town:** large population centers at strategically important locations, or waypoints and popular rest stops along harsh trails.

**City:** the largest and most important settlements of Drasil — capitals of small kingdoms, or the few successful hubs of a budding empire.

- These categories don't have to match the settlement's *name*: a prospering village can be treated as a town, and a crumbling city with squalid conditions and barely any resources — as a hamlet.

### Safe haven activities

Each activity takes **2 days** to complete and spends one **activity slot**. An activity can be chosen multiple times — each choice takes its own slot.

**Haggle:** choose one consumable item — it costs **2 inventory points less** the first time you acquire it.

**Secure provisions:** you gain rations totaling **size 20** before you need to start spending food.

**Smith & forge:** increase the **quality of one weapon** you carry by one stage. The same weapon cannot benefit twice (a weapon boosted to pristine cannot be boosted again to mastercraft until the upgrade is made permanent), and no weapon can go beyond mastercraft this way. The bonus lasts for as long as the weapon is maintained every 24 hours.

**Reinforce & improve:** pick one suit of armor — it gains one **trait** it doesn't already have (giving plate mail the *discrete* trait, say).

**Deep understanding:** you learn one **beginner spell** — through study, a deal, prayer, or connection to nature. No spell list restrictions, no prerequisites. You must meditate every 24 hours to retain the spell, and you can only hold one spell from this activity at a time.

**Harsh training:** you learn one **rank 1 fighting technique** — or a rank 2, if you already know a rank 1. You must train every 24 hours to keep the muscle memory, and you cannot hold more than one technique from this activity.

**Acquire connections:** the group regains **3 extra inventory points** the next time it reaches a safe haven.

**Complete rest:** the hero regains **all** resilience and mana.

**Partner up:** two or more heroes take this activity together, each spending a slot. Everyone who participates gains **+1d6 on all resist rolls** made with intellect, willpower or charisma — while another participant is within line of sight. The bonus doesn't stack from repeated takes, and you must *raise morale* every 24 hours to keep it. Pets and non-player allies can partner up too.

**Increased proficiency:** choose one talent with a *once per combat* or *once per day* activation — you may activate it a **second time** within that period. Once used, the benefit is spent.

- *You may also convert slots into camp time: every activity slot spent this way gives you 12 hours of camp activities.*

### Safe haven locations

Where a safe haven stands changes what it can offer.

**Desert:** desert havens share two drawbacks — *secure provisions* only covers the next day, and *haggle* only reduces the cost by 1. They come in two kinds:

- **Outpost:** supplies food for only **half** the usual time, refills no inventory points, and *acquire connections* gives nothing here.
- **Oasis:** you spend no inventory points on water or food — the oasis provides everything (applies only to a hamlet).

**Mountain:** built around smithing and mountain resources. *Smith & forge* raises a weapon's quality by **2 stages** (up to mastercraft), and *reinforce & improve* adds **two traits**. But *acquire connections* only yields 1 inventory point at the next haven.

**Grasslands:** the farmlands feed generously — food is supplied for longer (4 days for a hamlet, 12 for a village, 20 for a town, 50 for a city), and *acquire connections* grants 2 additional inventory points.

**Forest:** settlements of hunters and survivors. With *secure provisions*, you may spend 2 inventory points to take the activity **twice** on one slot.

**Coastal:** *smith & forge* and *reinforce & improve* cannot raise quality or add traits here — but they **can repair** the permanent quality, mobility and defense reductions caused by coastal travel.

**Swamp:** the climate interferes with the flow of mana — *deep understanding* is unavailable.

**Sky-cloud:** the heavenly winds nauseate the unadjusted. You cannot take *complete rest* unless you spent the last month above cloud level.

**Underwater:** most underwater settlements can host non-water-breathers — barely. You cannot take *increased proficiency* if you cannot breathe underwater.

### Safe haven variants

Not every safe haven is a rural village or a medieval city — some are specialized:

**Trade center** *(any type except hamlet)*: an important node of local trade. *Haggle* works on **two items** per slot, and *acquire connections* gives **each member** 2 inventory points instead of 3 for the whole group.

**Crime ridden** *(any type)*: bandits or crime lords run the place — or half the population are criminals.

- *Haggle:* a thief may try to steal the item you just haggled for (sense check, test score 2 to catch them — 3 in a crime-ridden town or city). If they succeed, the item is unavailable until the next safe haven.
- *Acquire connections:* there is a 50% chance your supplies are confiscated by outlaws and delivered to your **enemy**, who gains a pristine or mastercraft item. No enemy? The equipment is simply lost.

**Fortress** *(any type)*: held by professional soldiers, mercenaries or warriors. To get supplies, the heroes must persuade the commander (base test score 2, personality may raise it) — on a failure, the haven refills no inventory points (though *acquire connections* still works). In return:

- *Smith & forge* additionally gives the weapon a new trait — or removes one it has. Lost together with the quality bonus.
- *Reinforce & improve* additionally raises the armor's mobility by 1 stage (low → medium → high → unarmored). Lost together with the new trait.

**High magic** *(city or town)*: stronger magical ties than usual — a root of mana nearby, or a large population of magic users. *Haggling* for items of **rare** rarity or higher reduces their cost by 3 instead of 2, and one permanent item with the *magical* tag acquired here costs 1 less permanent inventory point.

**Primal** *(any type)*: no modern technology. *Smith & forge* and *reinforce & improve* are unavailable; *secure provisions* requires a sense check (test score 2); you cannot *haggle* for spell scrolls — but haggle works **twice** on poisons, traps and health potions.

**Monastery:** a solitary place of focus and self-improvement. Food is always provided, no matter how long you stay. You cannot *acquire connections*, and you regain 1 inventory point less than normal — but *harsh training* teaches **2 techniques**, *deep understanding* teaches **2 beginner spells**, *partner up* grants **1d8** instead of 1d6, and *training an attribute* gives **double the dice**.

**Abandoned:** a deserted haven has little to offer. It counts as one type lower (an abandoned city is a town), and *acquire connections*, *haggle* and *secure provisions* are unavailable.

## Equipment

This chapter will cover:

- Carrying capacity: how many sharp sticks can I carry?
- How does equipment work: what exactly do my items do?
- Weapons: how to use your sharp stick
- Armor: how do I avoid the sharp stick hurting that much?
- Other items: what else is there, other than a sharp stick? (full list)
- Magic items: what is special about them?

### Carrying capacity: how many sharp sticks can I carry?

All important equipment is written on your character sheet. Your carrying capacity has two parts: **inventory spaces** for permanent gear, and **inventory points** for everything else.

**Inventory spaces.** You start with a base of **3 inventory spaces**. For every die you have in **power**, you gain +1 space; for every die in **endurance**, +2 spaces — or +3, if your endurance die is a d10 or above. The sum from all sources is your **total inventory score**, and it is the maximum space you have: weapons, armor and other permanent items each take up at least 1 space.

**Inventory points.** In addition to permanent gear, you carry a number of inventory points — every character starts with **3**, and gains more through certain talent trees or by spending talent points. You spend inventory points during the adventure to gain consumable items: a health potion, rope, a good meal. In combat, converting points into an item is a *change the scene* move. You cannot convert what you don't have.

- *Optional rule — group inventory:* add up the inventory points of every hero into one big pile. When someone converts points into items, they are deducted from the group inventory instead of a personal one. When it hits 0, no one can convert.

### How does equipment work?

**Retrieving and buying items.** There are two ways to turn inventory into items:

**Buying:** you must be in a safe haven, or find a traveling merchant on the road. Buying permanent gear takes up the listed amount of your inventory spaces. You may only buy **standard quality** weapons, armor and shields, and **common** items. Pristine equipment and rare items require the Qg's approval beforehand (such as declaring the town has access to them); mastercraft and legendary equipment, and unique items, are only found as rewards — dungeons, raids, quests.

- The system does not really deal with money: it assumes the heroes have enough funds to keep adventuring, or take jobs in safe havens to cover their purchases.

**Retrieving:** only items — never weapons, armor or shields. All consumables can be retrieved, as can some permanent items (those list a retrieve cost). Retrieving is a special action, taken at the start of any round or in the middle of any scene: you declare that your character either acquired the item earlier and never mentioned it — or luckily found it at the pivotal moment.

**What can I do with my equipment?**

Basically? Whatever you want. The item's quality decides the bonus it gives. Unless stated otherwise, using a consumable item costs **1 move**; using a permanent item costs a move only if the action requires a roll, or directly affects another character (like emptying a waterskin onto a burning ally).

**What are the qualities of items?**

There are 5 quality tiers:

| Quality | Bonus | What it looks like |
|---|---|---|
| **Poor** | none | broken or long-worn items: old rope, a chipped dagger |
| **Standard** | +1d6 | most items in the game: a shortsword, a backpack |
| **Pristine** | +1d10 | some of the finest around: a finely crafted longsword, a camouflage cape |
| **Mastercraft** | +1d12, usually an extra ability | the magnum opus of famous crafters: a rapier that fires gusts of cutting air |
| **Legendary** | +2d8, always a unique ability | one of a kind, never replicated: a weightless halberd that still carries momentum — and shoots lightning |

A weapon that is not maintained properly counts as **1 quality level lower** than it actually is — legendary items excepted. Yes, that means an unmaintained mastercraft item can lose its special ability.

**What about items that hurt others without an attack roll?**

Bear traps, chains and the like restrain or hurt creatures without anyone rolling an attack. Such items carry a **test score** in their description: raise it by 1 for every quality tier above standard, lower it by 1 for every tier below (to a minimum of 0 — some poor items simply always fail, or, if the victim needs a move to check against them, automatically let them succeed).

**Are there different types of items?**

Three: **weapons, defensive and other**. Quality works differently for defensive equipment (armor and shields) — details in that section below.

- Some items grant no roll bonus at all — instead giving temporary buffs, negative conditions or one-time abilities. For these, quality indicates the power of the effect.
- Items that are not weapons or armor generally never reach legendary quality, and are not written in the item list at the end of this chapter.

### Weapons: how to use your sharp stick

**What are weapons?**

A weapon in Rise of Mortals is anything that can cause damage. Most of the time that means conventional weapons — axes, bows — but improvised weapons and unarmed strikes count too.

**Why do I need a weapon, then?**

Each weapon has traits that make it unique. Improvised weapons and unarmed strikes generally add no dice to the attack roll — making them less accurate and less deadly.

**Can I wield two weapons?**

Absolutely. You benefit from **both weapons' effective reach**: wielding a dagger and a spear, you deny the reach bonus to any attacker whose reach doesn't beat *both*.

**How do I attack with two weapons?**

Unless a talent says otherwise, you pick **one** weapon for each hostile move, use the normal attack rules, and gain no benefit against enemies who are only within the second weapon's reach.

**What can I dual wield?**

Either two short reach weapons — or one medium reach weapon and one *light*, short reach weapon.

#### Basic traits

Every weapon has the following traits.

**Ranged / melee:** each weapon is one or the other — this specifies the type of attack rolls it makes.

**Reach** *(melee weapons)*: the maximum distance, in meters, an attack with this weapon can target.

- **Short reach** — smaller weapons: daggers, knives, small clubs. Effective at 1 meter or less.
- **Medium reach** — the range of most weapons: warhammers, short spears, shortswords. Effective up to 2 meters. Can be held in two hands, but doesn't require it.
- **Long reach** — typically polearms: halberd, pike, lance or glaive — but also massive weapons like the war sword or pole axe. Effective up to 3 meters. Must be wielded with both hands: no extra weapons, no shield.

Three rules that come with reach:

- **Maximum reach** is the furthest point a weapon touches — a medium reach weapon has a maximum reach of 2 meters.
- **Two hands:** holding a weapon in two hands means those hands hold nothing else — no second weapon, shield or potion. In exchange, add **one more power die** to the attack roll (or +1d4, if you attack with speed).
- **Outreaching:** if you make a melee attack from outside the target's own melee reach, you gain **+1d6** to the roll. The same bonus applies against a target holding a ranged weapon or casting a spell.

**Range** *(ranged weapons)*: ranged attacks cannot benefit from effects that increase range, unless a feature specifically targets ranged weapons. A ranged weapon has two ranges:

- **Optimal range** — how accurately you fire, determined by your own senses: take your **sense or intellect** attribute — whichever of the two serves you better, the same way attacks choose between power and speed — and multiply the die's maximum value by the number of dice. A character with 1d6 sense has an optimal range of 6 meters; 2d10 intellect gives 20.
- **Maximum range** — listed in the weapon's description: the furthest a target can be and still be attacked.

Firing distance shapes the roll:

| Distance | Modifier |
|---|---|
| Within optimal range | **+1d8** to the roll |
| Up to twice the optimal range | **−1 success** |
| Up to maximum range | **−3 successes** |

#### Specific traits

Not every weapon has these — each carries a few, listed in its entry.

**Brutal:** the weapon deals **+1 damage** against creatures with at least 1 negative condition — **+2**, if the creature has 3 or more.

**Thrown:** made especially for throwing, but usable in melee — the weapon has both a reach and a range. Its maximum range is calculated by either method, your pick:

- equal to your **optimal range**, or
- equal to your **power dice size × number of power dice, +1**.

Thrown weapons count as both melee and ranged, and can use either power or speed for hostile moves.

**Hidden:** checks to conceal the weapon gain **+1d10** — and so do attacks made with it against **unaware** enemies.

**Deadly:** kills even from a glancing blow. You can deal **more than 1 damage using speed** with this weapon.

**Defensive** *(melee only)*: designed to guard against weapons of similar reach. When a creature standing within your weapon's reach attacks you, you gain **+1 evasion** against it.

**Thrusting:** made to stab and pierce — the weapon's reach increases by **1**, and you gain the outreaching attack bonus even against creatures of **equal** reach.

**Forceful:** heavy momentum behind every strike — the weapon pushes the target back a number of meters equal to the damage it took.

**Light:** hits with less force: if the target uses *guard*, the attack's damage automatically drops to 0. In exchange, a light weapon is easy to handle — whatever its reach, you may always hold it in one hand.

**Bladed:** extremely deadly to unarmored targets (a creature with at least −1 damage reduction counts as armored). Against the unarmored, the weapon deals damage equal to the **number of dice rolled** instead of the number of successes — you still need to hit by the normal rules, and speed still caps at 1 damage unless the weapon is also *deadly*.

**Armor breaker:** attacks with this weapon **ignore damage reduction**.

**Armor destroyer:** made to shatter every defense: attacks ignore damage reduction **and any tags the armor gives its wearer**.

**Chain:** built for complex maneuvers — when targeting a creature within your reach, add the weapon's **quality dice** to any impairing attempt to restrain or knock the creature prone by means other than an attack.

**Slow:** devastating, but takes time to aim. Attacking costs **two moves**: the *wind up* must be the **last** move of your turn, and the *hit* (where you roll the attack) must be the **first** move of your next turn. The attack converts every success into **2 damage** instead of 1 — and if it uses speed, the base 1 damage is doubled too. Skip the hit on the following round, and the wind up must be retaken.

**Reload:** the weapon has an ammo mechanism, written as **X;Y** — X shots before reloading, Y moves to reload (a 6;2 weapon fires 6 shots, then takes 2 moves to reload). At mastercraft quality or above, reduce the reload time by 1.

**Mechanical:** the weapon cannot use **power** for its attacks — and has a minimum damage floor: after all damage is calculated, if the total is below the weapon's **mechanical score**, it deals the mechanical score instead. Pristine quality raises the score by 1; mastercraft or legendary — by 2.

**Swirl:** when used to *whirlwind*, the attack gains **+1 success**.

- *A note on extra damage:* the additional damage from traits like brutal or slow is added only **after a hit is confirmed, but before damage reduction** — and it bypasses the 1-damage cap of speed attacks.

#### Weapon list

##### Melee — short reach

**Throwing knife** — a small blade on a one-handed hilt, easy to hide, deadly at short range. *Traits: thrown, deadly, hidden, light.*

**Tanto** — a very short sword, usually carried alongside a longer weapon. *Traits: bladed, deadly, light.*

**Gauntlet** — steel gloves that leave your hands free for another weapon, but serve well in self-defense. *Traits: defensive, brutal.*

**Parrying dagger** — made especially to deflect the blows of longer weapons, and to thrust. *Traits: thrusting, defensive, light.*

**Tomahawk** — a throwing axe with a thin blade: easy to make, quick to draw. *Traits: bladed, thrown.*

**Unarmed strikes** — every creature's natural weapon; rarely the perfect one. *Traits: light.* Special: has no quality, and takes up no inventory space.

##### Melee — medium reach

**Arming sword** — a balanced sword for training or war, equally fit for attack and defense. *Traits: bladed, deadly.*

**Flail** — a spiked ball on a short chain, built for brutal swings. *Traits: chain, swirl, brutal.*

**Warhammer** — made to pierce armor and destroy shields with maximum efficiency, one-handed. *Traits: armor breaker, brutal.*

**Mace** — blunt, deadly force; very effective against small or armored creatures. *Traits: forceful, brutal.*

**Short-spear** — a small polearm, commonly paired with a shield, excellent at keeping enemies at bay. *Traits: thrusting, thrown, light, defensive.*

**Falchion** — a dangerous sword that ruins any unarmored target; frightening on every battlefield. *Traits: bladed, swirl, brutal.*

**Rapier** — a fencing weapon for thrusting and parrying — heavier than it looks. *Traits: bladed, thrusting, defensive, light.* Special: cannot be held in two hands.

**Bearded axe** — a one-handed axe with a long head, built for wide, brutal swings that cut flesh like butter. *Traits: bladed, armor breaker.*

**Fighting staff** — a sturdy, straight wooden staff; a favorite of farmers and monks. *Traits: light, forceful, swirl, defensive.*

##### Melee — long reach

**War spear** — a two-handed pole with a spearhead, great against cavalry. *Traits: thrusting, defensive.*

**Glaive** — a polearm with a long blade, capable of massive damage. *Traits: armor breaker, bladed.*

**Whip** — a leather strap on a short hilt: extravagant slashes from afar. *Traits: light, chain, swirl.*

**War sword** — a massive two-handed blade, hard to control for its almost unwieldy weight. *Traits: bladed, defensive, swirl.*

**Maul** — a massive sledgehammer, wielded only by the most powerful warriors. *Traits: slow, brutal, armor destroyer.*

**Lance** — the cavalry weapon: the sheer force of impact sometimes shatters it. *Traits: brutal, slow, forceful, thrusting.* Special: while mounted, its *slow* trait becomes optional.

**Halberd** — an almost 2-meter axe with a spike at the end, terrifying force behind each swing. *Traits: brutal, bladed, thrusting.*

**Lucerne hammer** — less a weapon, more a wrecking ball: raised high, then brought down with astonishing power. *Traits: armor breaker, forceful, brutal.*

##### Ranged weapons

**Sling** — a simple weapon for flinging rocks and bullets. *Range: 30 m. Traits: light, hidden.* Its projectiles count as both ranged and thrown weapons.

**Shortbow** — a lighter, less powerful cousin of the longbow. *Range: 150 m. Traits: deadly.*

**Longbow** — pierces flesh like butter, and sometimes armor too. *Range: 250 m. Traits: bladed.*

**Warbow** — a brutal weapon of war against unarmored targets and armored knights alike. *Range: 300 m. Traits: slow.* Special — *heavy draw weight*: the bow's optimal range is dictated by the character's **power** instead of sense, while attack rolls still use sense.

**Crossbow** — an advanced weapon made to break through armor. *Range: 350 m. Reload: 1;2. Traits: deadly, armor breaker, mechanical (2).*

**Hand crossbow** — smaller, more elegant, more discreet. *Range: 70 m. Traits: hidden, mechanical (1).* Special: can be shot **and reloaded with one hand** — hand crossbows can be dual wielded.

**Revolver** — an experimental firearm holding up to 6 bullets, at the cost of power. *Range: 200 m. Reload: 6;2. Traits: deadly, mechanical (2).*

**Bolt action** — an extremely powerful firearm that tears through armor and shield alike. *Range: 450 m. Reload: 1;3. Traits: deadly, armor destroyer, mechanical (4).*

**Double barrel shotgun** — brutal at short range; pierces stone up close. *Range: 25 m. Reload: 2;2. Traits: deadly, forceful, mechanical (3).*

- *A note on holding ranged weapons:* ranged weapons are not two-handed weapons, but you cannot hold a shield or another weapon in your off hand and still use one — they take one hand to hold and **two hands to fire**. The exception is the revolver, fired with one hand — though, like every reloading weapon, it takes two hands to reload.

##### Special weapons

**Hand mortar** — a ranged weapon that doesn't roll to hit: instead, every creature within a **2-meter radius** of the targeted point makes a speed resist roll against the damage. *Range: 70 m. Reload: 1;6. Traits: fire, mechanical (damage based on quality).*

| Quality | Damage |
|---|---|
| Poor | 2 |
| Standard | 4 |
| Pristine | 6 |
| Mastercraft | 8 |
| Legendary | 11 |

**Pike** — the longest melee weapon: an almost unwieldy 3-meter staff with a sharp spike. *Reach: 5 meters. Traits: thrusting, brutal.* Cannot attack enemies 2 meters or closer.

**Branch of the world tree** — a hardy branch taken from the tree of creation. *Reach: 5 meters. Traits: forceful; may add slow at will.* Cannot attack enemies 2 meters or closer, and requires a wielder of **size 4 or larger**.

- If you end your turn without attacking, you may charge the branch with spiritual energy. The next time it hits a **heavily wounded** creature, that creature makes a resist roll (rolling its endurance and willpower dice combined) against the attack's damage as the test score — on a failure, it is **knocked out**.

Both special melee weapons take **3 inventory spaces** to carry.

##### Weapon variants

*Named takes on the weapons above — every one of them mastercraft, every one of them found, not bought.*

**Hand mortar "Shameful Dragon"** — made by demons to bring doom and destruction. Forged in fire and flame, its ammunition burns the very ground into scorching land — and every shot sounds like the roar of a dragon.

- *Quality: mastercraft. Range: 90 m. Reload: 1;6. Traits: fire, mechanical (6). Hits a 4-meter radius instead of 2.*
- **Scorching ground:** the area hit by the Shameful Dragon melts and burns — it becomes dangerous terrain, and a creature that starts its turn on it takes 2 fire damage. The scorching ground lasts until the end of the current round.

**Double barrel shotgun "Lead Cloud"** — an engineering beauty: someone decided a double barrel wasn't deadly enough against unarmored targets at close range. It has a two-hammer system but uses a single bullet — and somehow works without fail. The only explanation experts offer is "magic".

- *Quality: mastercraft. Range: 5 m. Reload: 1;2. Traits: deadly, forceful, hidden, mechanical (5).*
- **Lead-Shredder:** the grapeshot bullets shred through skin and meat, but are useless against plate. Attacks gain **+5 damage against unarmored** targets — and drop to *mechanical (1)* against armored ones.
- **Special move — Blackout:** overload the two-hammer system and fire a "false shot". The bullet explodes immediately, raising a dust cloud in a **5-meter radius** around you: creatures inside it take 1 armor destroying damage and are **blinded**, and the cloud provides abstract cover until the end of the round. After a blackout, the next reload is 1;3 instead of 1;2.

**Bolt action rifle "Sitting Duck"** — bulky and large at first glance, difficult to handle even for giants. Its owner was clearly a hunter who took good care of it and made some special improvements. But now it is in your hands — I wonder how it got to you?

- *Quality: mastercraft. Inventory spaces: 2. Range: 500 m. Reload: 1;4. Traits: deadly, armor destroyer, mechanical (4).*
- **Nest-y place** — new action, *Make nest (3 moves)*: your movement drops to 0. In exchange, you gain an **additional sense die**, your reload becomes 1;2, and your shots turn silent — they do not break stealth. The price of comfort: you gain a blind spot covering the first third of your optimal range (rounded up), your evasion drops by 3, and your power, speed and endurance resist rolls take −3. To stand back up, use *Make nest* again — all bonuses and penalties end. If anything moves you, you exit the nest automatically and the weapon counts as dropped.

**Bearded axe "Bloodtooth"** — not just a battleaxe, but a container for one of the dead god's powerful abominations, forged so that no attack, magical or mundane, could break the creature's prison. They say if you listen closely, you can hear a soft whisper coming from the blade.

- *Quality: mastercraft. Inventory spaces: 1. Traits: bladed, armor breaker.*
- **Legacy of the Black Blood:** *"If something exists, I'll make it bleed to death."* If a Bloodtooth attack causes bleeding, it ignores immunity to bleed and immunity to bleed's continual damage. For such is the will of Bloodtooth.
- **New actions** *(require holding the axe in both hands; each automatically applies the* draw blood *technique, if the character knows it — and cannot be used if your resilience is equal to or below the action's cost)*:
  - **Bloody Spiral** *(3 moves, 3 resilience)*: a circular attack that releases the axe's power, sprinkling blood everywhere. You attack every creature within your reach; the attack gains *thrusting* and inflicts **blinded** (until the start of the next turn). You are blinded too.
  - **Gruesome torture** *(2 moves, 4 resilience)*: you let Bloodtooth take control and unleash its rage. The attack gains **+2d8** and inflicts **weakened** (until the end of the next turn) and **intimidated** (test score 1 to remove); the target's movement is halved. You are weakened as well (until the end of the next turn).
  - **Bloodpath** *(1 move, 2 resilience)*: you listen to the whispers and let them lead you: teleport up to **15 meters** to a point within line of sight. Teleporting into an occupied point deals you damage equal to the number of occupied spaces you landed in, and pushes you to the nearest free space.
- **Hungry Maw:** Bloodtooth cannot be enchanted — it *feeds*. The axe accepts only the **Vampiric** enchantment, and it does not take it: it devours it. Feed Bloodtooth an item carrying the Vampiric enchantment, and that item loses its enchantment **forever**. Each feeding grants a boon — and after the third, the axe's hunger is sated, and it can never be enchanted or fed again:
  1. Bloodtooth's actions cannot reduce your resilience below half its maximum.
  2. Bloodtooth's actions stop dealing damage to you.
  3. Bloodtooth's actions no longer inflict negative conditions on you.

### Staffs and other stuff

This section covers the equipment that assists casters in performing magic.

**Does equipment restrict my casting?**

Depending on your type of spellcasting, you may need to avoid armor and shields to cast effectively. Most weapons neither help nor hurt your casting — but holding a two-handed weapon makes you unable to use a spell focus.

**Do I need a spell focus to cast?**

No — bare hands work. But a spell focus (a magical staff, say) can increase your magical ability, and it's generally the better way to cast.

**What is a spell focus, and what does it do?**

A spell focus is a unique type of equipment that helps its user cast spells — anything from a simple staff to a crystal ball. You add the focus's **quality dice** to rolls made with spells, and each focus carries a unique benefit. If your focus is of mastercraft quality, it can be awarded the effect of one more focus.

- *Important: a spell can only be empowered by 1 spell focus at any time.*

#### Spell focus list

**Freeze focus** — deeply connected to the element of frost and cold. When your spell reduces a creature's movement to zero, you deal 1 damage to it.

**Flame focus** — connected to the element of flame. When you cast a spell that deals damage, it applies 1 continual fire damage — or, if it already deals continual damage, increases that damage by 1 instead.

**Electric focus** — adds the power of lightning to your spells. When a creature is shocked as a result of your spell with the electric tag, you may shock another creature within 10 meters of the target.

**Pure focus** — made to help sustain a magic user's mana mid-combat. You can make attacks using the same attribute you cast spells with, adding the focus's quality to the roll. *Range: 30 meters. Damage tags: mana drain.*

**Vampiric focus** — a dark focus that takes the vitality of others to keep its user healthy. When you knock out or **kill** a creature using a spell, you regain 1 resilience.

**Sustaining focus** — made to sustain continued effects: your spells that are not instant stay in effect for 1 more round.

**Defensive focus** — made to protect its user: if you finish casting a spell on your turn, you gain **−1 damage reduction** until the start of your next turn.

**Supportive focus** — bolsters the life energy of those it touches. When you target a creature with a spell, you may empower it: **+5 movement** and **+1d8 to its next roll**. If the spell targets several creatures, you choose one.

**Conjurer's focus** — reinforces what your spells create: walls summoned through it gain +5 resilience, and summoned creatures gain +2 unphased and +1d6 to their rolls.

### Armor: how do I avoid the sharp stick hurting that much?

**How does armor work?**

Armor, depending on its classification (light, medium or heavy), increases the wearer's **maximum unphased** — and heavier armor also grants **damage reduction**. When choosing armor at character creation, heroes can customize their own using the *armor creation process*, detailed later in this chapter.

#### Basic armor traits

Every armor has these four values:

- **Defense:** the unphased added to the wearer.
- **Trait count:** the maximum number of specific traits the armor can carry.
- **Damage reduction:** how much damage the armor shrugs off.
- **Mobility:** how comfortably you move in it. Three levels:

**Low mobility** — the armor limits its user in major ways:

- −1 success on power or speed checks made to jump, swim or climb, and on endurance checks to run for long periods.
- You can only stride **once** each turn.
- −1 success on ranged weapon attacks.
- Your base evasion is **halved** (rounded up).
- Takes **2 inventory spaces**.

**Medium mobility** — moves fairly easily, but still limits you:

- Base evasion reduced by 1 (to a minimum of 1).
- −1 success on ranged weapon attacks.
- Takes **1 inventory space**.

**High mobility** — almost limitless movement: no penalties at all. Takes **0 inventory spaces**.

Three rules that apply to all armor:

- An unpolished armor drops its mobility rank by 1 — and if it is already at low, the wearer cannot jump, swim or climb until it's polished.
- All armor gives **−1 success on stealth checks**.
- Wearing **no armor at all** has its own reward: **+5 movement** and **+1d6 to all hostile and resist rolls**.

#### Specific armor traits

**Plating:** the armor reduces the damage from *bladed* attacks to 1.

**Padding:** the armor ignores the *armor breaker* tag — if the weapon is not bladed.

**Reinforced:** attacks against this armor gain no bonus damage from *brutal*, and can reduce a *mechanical* weapon's damage below its baseline.

**Weighted:** when the wearer is pushed back, the distance is reduced by half.

**Discrete:** this armor does not hinder the wearer's stealth checks.

**Spiked:** creatures attacking you from short reach gain bleed ranks equal to the armor's quality — poor: 1, standard: 2, pristine: 4, mastercraft: 5, legendary: 7.

**Utility belt:** grants 1 inventory point that can be used to retrieve any standard consumable item.

**Back pack:** the wearer can carry creatures up to a total of their own size, with no penalties.

**Insulated:** choose fire, frost or electric — you take half damage (rounded down) from continual damage of that type.

**Robust** *(requires 2 trait slots)*: AoE damage no longer ignores your damage reduction.

**Cushioned:** you count as being 2 sizes smaller for the purposes of fall damage (to a minimum of size 1).

#### Armor creation

When creating armor, choose one of three templates (all of standard quality — and armor gained at character creation must be standard):

| Template | Defense | Trait count | Mobility | Damage reduction |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| **Light armor** | 0 | 2 | high | 0 |
| **Medium armor** | 1 | 1 | medium | −1 |
| **Heavy armor** | 2 | 2 | low | −1 |

#### Armor quality changes

**Light armor** — poor: only 1 trait. Pristine: +1 defense. Mastercraft: +1 more defense, and +1 trait count.

**Medium armor** — poor: −1 defense. Pristine: +1 trait count. Mastercraft: +2 defense.

**Heavy armor** — poor: the armor's specific traits lose their effect (the −1 damage reduction remains). Pristine: +2 defense. Mastercraft: +1 damage reduction (to a total of −2).

### Shields: added self protection

**What are shields for?**

Shields are a very important part of the Rise of Mortals combat system. On your turn, you may spend a move to **raise your shield**: while raised, it adds its defensive value to your **evasion** — and can even take attacks meant for you.

- The shield stays raised until you lower it, make a hostile move, or finish casting a spell.
- If you make a hostile move while your shield is raised, you cannot raise it again that turn.
- **Once per round**, when an attack hits you while your shield is raised, you may **redirect it to the shield**. The shield has no damage reduction of its own (unless it has the *sturdy* trait), and attacks hitting a shield lose the *bladed* trait.
- When a shield's resilience hits 0, it **breaks**: a broken shield cannot redirect attacks and must be repaired first. Raising a broken shield adds only **half its defensive value** (rounded down) to your evasion.

**What weapons work with a shield?**

Any medium or short reach weapon fits in the other hand. Of the long reach weapons, only those with the *light* trait can be paired with a shield.

#### Basic shield traits

- **Defensive value:** what you add to your evasion when the shield is raised.
- **Shield resilience:** how much damage the shield takes before breaking.

#### Specific shield traits

**Sturdy (X):** the shield itself has damage reduction — redirected attacks deal it X less damage.

**Big:** covers a wide range: it always adds **+1 evasion even unraised** — but takes **2 moves** to raise.

**Small:** small enough to hold without focus, at the cost of coverage: its defensive value does not apply against **ranged** attacks. While unraised, the shield hand can still hold a small object or a short reach weapon — and once per round, you may redirect a **melee** attack to the shield even while it is not raised.

#### Shield list

**Buckler** — a small shield meant for parrying blows; less effective against arrows. *Defensive value: 1. Resilience: 3. Traits: small, sturdy (2).*

**Heater shield** — a triangular metal shield: a good all-rounder for foot soldiers and mobile adventurers. *Defensive value: 2. Resilience: 5.*

**Kite shield** — a large shield of great protection against all attacks, the choice of knights. *Defensive value: 2. Resilience: 4. Traits: big, sturdy (1).*

**Spiked shield** — a heater variation built to hit back. *Defensive value: 1. Resilience: 4. Traits: brutal.*

- *A note on attacking with shields:* shields are not built to attack, so all shields count as **light weapons of poor quality** — except the spiked shield, which adds its actual quality to attacks.

**Shield quality:** every quality increase **doubles** the shield's resilience; mastercraft and legendary shields also gain +1 defensive value. Poor shields lose 1 defensive value.

### Other equipment: what else is there?

Rise of Mortals has plenty of items that are neither weapons, armor nor shields — equipment can take almost any form.

**What is the currency of Rise of Mortals?**

**Inventory points are the economy.** They are money, barter and preparation all at once: need a rope? You have one — for a point. The coins of Drasil — bronze *peasant's gifts* ("gits") and the silver-gold *king's credentials* ("kics", called *bastards* by the common folk, worth about 50 gits each) — exist in the world and in its stories, but the system abstracts all of it into inventory points.

Two general notes:

- Attacking with equipment that is not a weapon still deals damage — but you do not add the item's quality to the attack roll.
- An item that reaches **mastercraft** quality (through crafting or *smith & forge*) may gain one enchantment from the list at the end of this chapter. No item can become **legendary** through upgrades.

#### Equipment cost

**Weapons:**

- Short reach: 2 weapons per 1 inventory space.
- Medium reach: 1 space.
- Long reach, or ranged weapon + ammunition: 2 spaces (a sling needs no ammo slot).
- **Bundles:** you may carry up to 10 of the same small weapon as a bundle — it takes 2 spaces, and a move to draw from in combat.

**Armor:** high mobility — 0 spaces, medium — 1, low — 2.

**Shields:** big — 2 spaces, all others — 1.

**Common permanent items:**

- **Backpack** — 2 spaces; grants **+2 inventory points**. Once per character.
- **20 meters of rope** — 1 space.
- **Bundle of 5 torches** — 1 space.
- **Tent** — 3 spaces; accommodates up to a total of size 5.
- **Oil lantern** — 1 space.
- **Oil sack (3.5 liters)** — 1 space.

**Equipment kits** *(2 spaces each)*: a kit adds its quality to its related rolls — never to hostile rolls or rolls made as a result of casting a spell.

- **Hunter's kit** — searching for food, making traps.
- **Traveler's kit** — climbing, digging, woodcutting.
- **Magician's kit** — identifying magical effects.
- **Burglar's kit** — disarming traps, picking locks.
- **Craftsman's kit** — crafting items and traps.
- **Survivor's kit** — hiding, healing others.
- **Wayfinder's kit** — tracking others, disarming traps.

**Transportation:** for a comfortable ride, your mount should be 1 size larger than you. A standard quality mount can carry its rider and has a total inventory of 10 (including the rider's); every quality increase adds 3 to its max inventory points. A poor quality mount has 6 total inventory. <!-- Скорости по качествам осознанно не вписаны (значения ещё не выбраны); логика будет как у travel values из главы Traveling -->

#### Traps

Unique rules: pristine and mastercraft traps can only be retrieved in a safe haven. If a creature didn't see you place a trap, it must succeed on a **sense check** to notice it — test score 2 for poor and standard quality, 3 for pristine and above.

**Bear trap** *(standard — 1 inventory point to retrieve)*: 3 moves to place on a 1×1 meter space. A creature of size 4 or smaller stepping in makes a **speed resist check** (test score 2 in standard condition) — on a failure it is **restrained**, takes 1 damage and 2 ranks of bleed. Escaping takes a move and a **power check** (test score 1); failing the escape triggers the damage and bleed again.

- *Poor:* the speed check's test score drops to 1, and creatures cannot fail the escape check.
- *Pristine:* the escape test score rises to 2; the trap deals 3 damage and 5 bleed ranks.
- *Mastercraft:* both test scores become 4, and the trap catches creatures up to size 6.

**Tripwire trap** *(standard — 2 inventory points to retrieve)*: 4 moves to set up a 1×5 meter line. A creature of size 3 or smaller stepping through **falls prone**.

- *Poor:* covers 1×3 meters, trips size 2 or smaller.
- For every quality **above** standard: +2 to the size of creatures affected, +4 meters of line.

**Other traps:** most cannot be retrieved directly and must be built. If the trap deals damage or inflicts a condition, gather materials first — spend inventory points, or roll **sense** to scavenge the area: every point spent or success rolled adds **1 damage** to the trap (or 5 meters of drop, for falling traps). Then roll an **intellect check** to craft it. A trap covers a 1×2 meter line of your choice — vertical, horizontal, or flat on the ground — and every 15 minutes of focus extends it 1 meter in one direction. Unless stated otherwise, building a trap without focusing takes 1 minute.

#### Consumables

- **Minor potion of health** *(1 point)*: regain up to 3 resilience.
- **Minor potion of mana** *(1 point)*: regain up to 5 mana.
- **Potion of lesser power** *(2 points)*: gain the *lesser strength* and *lesser dexterity* spells' benefits for 1 minute.
- **Beginner spell scroll** *(3 points)*: one cast of any beginner spell — 0 mana, 1 move to read.
- **Vial of poison** *(1 point)*: coat a *bladed* weapon, bow or crossbow. The next hit deals +1 damage and **poisons** the target for 1 minute.
- **Food** *(1 point)*: feeds a total of size 6 before running out — a size 2 creature eats three times, a bigger one manages twice.

**Pristine consumables** *(unlocked at* local hero *renown)*:

- **Potion of health** *(2 points)*: regain up to 7 resilience.
- **Potion of mana** *(2 points)*: regain up to 12 mana.
- **Potion of power** *(3 points)*: gain the *greater physique* and *increased reflexes* spells' benefits for 1 minute.
- **Advanced spell scroll** *(5 points)*: one cast of any advanced spell — 0 mana, 2 moves to read.
- **Vial of fireblood** *(2 points)*: a toxin that boils the blood. Laced on a melee or thrown weapon, the next successful attack poisons the target; on a ranged weapon, the next attack gains the poison tag. The poisoned creature takes 2 continual poison damage at the start of each round — and 1 poison damage every time it uses mana (an endurance check against a test score of **2** to remove).
- **A white lily petal** *(4 points)*: consumed as a move, it immediately cures the target of all poisons, removes all continual damage — and pulls them out of the **Ashen** state without any of its consequences.

### Magical items: what's different?

Magical items are very rare — the masterpiece of a blacksmith, or an ancient relic. They are always of **mastercraft quality or higher**, and almost never for sale.

**What can they do?**

A magical item usually carries 2 or 3 unique and powerful properties. The features can be anything, but we recommend the following guidelines:

- A magical **weapon** should have at least one property that raises its offensive power — more reach, a ranged attack, damage tags.
- A magical **armor or clothing** should grant a defensive buff or a new movement option — flight, waterbreathing.
- A magical **shield** should carry a once-per-day or once-per-battle defensive ability that can turn the tide.

Magic items are rare — but reward your players with one or two every once in a while, so the equipment grows alongside the characters.

#### Magic enchantment list

Typical enchantments placed on mastercraft items. A character cannot wear multiple enchanted items of the same type — no pairing a cape of protection with a tunic of camouflage, both being enchanted clothing.

**Frost weapon:** gains the *frost* damage tag on its first attack each round.

**Electric weapon:** gains the *electric* damage tag on its first attack each round — and deals 1 damage to its user after the attack resolves.

**Flaming weapon:** gains the *fire* and *continual fire damage (2)* tags.

**Vampiric weapon** *(melee)*: gains the *draining* tag.

**Holy weapon:** gains the *mana drain* tag, and inflicts **blinded** on a hit.

**Grasscutter** *(bladed weapon)*: grants a special melee attack — a wind blade with a range of **20 meters**, usable with power or speed. With power, the attack gains *forceful*; with speed, *deadly*.

**Emerald piercer** *(armor breaker or slow weapon)*: gains the *deadly* and *armor destroyer* traits.

**Earthcrusher** *(two-handed weapon)*: causes a shockwave on hit — every **creature** within 10 meters of the target takes 1 damage per success rolled, and is pushed 5 meters away from the target. The target itself and the user are **both spared**.

**Self aiming** *(ranged weapon)*: ignores cover and anything that denies you a clear shot — including your own blindness. It does not ignore conditions that hamper your ability to **physically** shoot, like weakened or poisoned.

**Explosive:** the weapon deals 2 damage to every creature within a 5-meter radius of the target on hit — target and user included, if they're in the area. Fire, frost and electric tags upgrade the blast:

- *Fire* — damage rises to 5, radius to 7 meters.
- *Frost* — damage drops to 1, radius to 2 meters; the explosion gains the *frost* tag and spares the original target.
- *Electric* — the explosion gains the *electric* tag.

**Missile deflection** *(armor, shield or clothing)*: **−2 damage reduction** against ranged attacks.

**Camouflage** *(clothing)*: shifts its colors to match the surroundings — add its quality to all checks made to hide, sneak or obscure your presence.

**Protection** *(jewelry or clothing)*: **+1 evasion**.

**Windstride** *(jewelry or clothing)*: grants **flight**. Flying distance is double your movement speed — but turning costs 5 meters.

**Swiftness** *(clothing or armor)*: **+30 meters** of movement.

**Attribute improvement** *(any equipment)*: tied to one predetermined attribute — it grants either **+1 dice** or **+2 dice size** to its wearer.

**Rapid strike** *(weapon)*: for the most proficient warriors only. After your **3rd attack in a round**, deal damage to all enemies within reach — 2 damage for every die of the attribute you attacked with.

**Vengeance** *(armor, shield or jewelry)*: a seal of dark magic. The first time you damage an enemy that targeted you this round, deal **+2 damage** and gain **3 temporary resilience**.

#### Legendary items

One of a kind — the knowledge to create them was lost long ago. Every legendary item has a name, and at least one extremely powerful, unique ability.

**Fimbulwinter, the bow of never ending frost** — created to combat the now-dead god of flames. A longbow with the *frost*, *self aiming* and *holy* enchantments; grants immunity to the fire damage tag, and deals **double damage** to creatures made of fire or bearing the *scion of the flame* enemy type.

**Emo and Shashou** — a pair of throwing daggers made to fight the demons of the moon.

- *Emo:* *electric* and *explosive*; while paired with Shashou, gains *vampiric*.
- *Shashou:* *missile deflection* and *swiftness*; while paired with Emo, gains *windstride*.
- Wielding both grants **−2 damage reduction** against demons and occult magic — and when Emo is thrown, it teleports back to Shashou after the attack resolves.

**Earthshaker** — a giant halberd forged to fight the giants of the old world. *Earthcrusher* and *flaming* enchantments; the earthcrusher's damage also carries the fire tag. While holding it, you can cast *Pillar strike* once per day and *Stone guillotine* once per battle — casting time reduced to 1 move, mana cost unchanged.

**Kaze-yari** — a spear made to combat the old dragon king and his kind. *Emerald piercer*, *protection* and *grasscutter* enchantments; creatures hit by it cannot fly for 1 minute, and wounds it makes cannot be healed for 24 hours.

**The hippo's teeth** — a maul with a self-contained mana engine, made by the land's most talented blacksmith as a gift for his lover — who died in battle right as he finished it. The weapon holds **15 charges**; at the start of the wielder's turn, spend any number:

- 1 charge: **+2 effective reach**.
- 2 charges: **+1d10** to the next hostile roll.
- 4 or more charges: gain the **ability to fly** and +15 meters of movement for 1 minute.

Effects of the same type don't stack within one activation. All charges return when the wielder sleeps — and you gain 3 charges whenever you regain mana.

**Inverted tool of heaven** — after the victory over the demons, it became painfully clear the angels had only ever used the mortals — and planned to be rid of them. This warhammer was made to fight angels: its user is immune to *mana drain*, and a creature hit by it loses **all benefits from talents, abilities and spells** until the start of your next turn.

**Helmet of the king killer** — an artifact drenched in the blood of 33 tyrants slain by the first spider knight. He gifted it to his apprentice after she killed him in single combat; a day later, the second massacre of monarchs began.

- The wearer can **kill a sentient creature to gain the benefits of 6 hours of sleep** — and cannot sleep normally at all.
- Takes 1 inventory space; carries the *vengeance* and *swiftness* enchantments.
- When the wearer moves, they **mark** any number of creatures that were adjacent to them at any point of the movement. A marked creature's evasion becomes **0**, and no effect can redirect damage away from it. The mark ends when the creature takes damage.
- The helmet can only be removed by **Ashen** creatures.

**Astronimical** — a tanto made from the spine of an archangel. *Traits: armor destroyer, mechanical (5), defensive.*

- To wield Astronimical fully, you must **convince it that you are worthy** (test score **7** to convince).
- While wielding it, you **cannot spend mana** — but you can cast all beginner and advanced level **illusion** spells with 1 move, at no cost.
- If you failed to convince it, you cannot make hostile moves with Astronimical.

## Magic: Glowing Rocks and the Like

In Rise of Mortals, magic is a common concept, wielded by many different creatures. Plenty of player characters will want it — as the core of their hero, or as a way to augment powers they already hold.

### What is magic?

Magic is the practice of harnessing mana — found in the world around you, or in your inner power — to perform unnatural feats of wonder. Mana manipulation takes two main forms: **spellcasting** and **enchanted items**.

**What is spellcasting?**

The most common form of mana manipulation: using mana to temporarily affect the environment and the creatures near the source of the manipulation. Creatures who cast spells are called mages, sorcerers and witches — general terms, born of common folk's inability to tell apart the four sources of spellcasting.

**What are the 4 sources of spellcasting?**

- **Arcane magic** — the practice of understanding the hidden formulas and rules that govern mana, and manipulating them into strong offensive capabilities, or into empowering others.
- **Occult magic** — a darker form: commanding, or bargaining with, demons and beings from dimensions beyond our understanding, in exchange for great power.
- **Holy magic** — focusing one's belief and willpower into magic that protects others and summons powerful beings to the caster's aid.
- **Natural magic** — the oldest form: being in tune with nature, understanding the world around you, and harnessing its power.

**What are enchanted items?**

Unlike spellcasting, an enchanted item has gone through a **permanent** change, and now holds abilities impossible without magic — constantly channeling and gathering mana to fuel them. An enchanted item can be anything from a magical sword to enchantingly beautiful jewelry.

**Can one craft enchanted items?**

Yes — but once a piece of equipment receives any enchantment, it is under that enchantment permanently: it cannot change it, and cannot gain another. Legendary items are the only equipment carrying more than one enchantment — and the knowledge of making them is lost to time. Fewer than **100 legendary items** exist in the whole world.

### How does magic work?

Characters can learn magic in many ways, but the main one is spellcasting. Both the players and the Qg should know its basics — it lets the players build better tactics, and the Qg make better rulings when disagreements come up.

Every spell has the following:

- **Base cost:** how much mana the spell costs to cast.
- **Base casting time:** how many moves the cast takes.
- **Base duration:** how long the spell's effect lasts.
- **Level:** beginner, advanced, master or legendary.

*A note on round-based durations:* the round a spell is cast on **counts** towards its duration. A spell lasting 15 seconds (1 round), cast on the last turn of a round, ends immediately.

**How can I cast a spell?**

You can only cast if you have taken the **beginner magic talent** and chosen one of the 4 sources. From then on, you can cast any spell you know. The process, every time, at the start of your turn:

1. Decide what spell you are casting. Not on your known spell list — not an option.
2. Before starting the cast, apply any talents or modifiers that fit.
3. Use the *start casting a spell* move, then take the required number of moves to finish. The starting move **counts**: a spell with a casting time of 1 move resolves immediately as you start it.
4. Unless the spell says otherwise, you pick its targets — or make its roll — on the **last** move of the cast.
5. The moment the spell is cast, you pay its mana cost.

**Can I cast with 0 mana?**

No — and you can't even *start* casting a spell if, at that moment, you don't have enough mana to pay for it. The cost itself is only paid when the cast finishes.

You cannot cast the same spell twice on the same turn.

**The order of spell levels:**

Some abilities refer to spell levels by rank order. It's simple: **beginner** is the lowest; **advanced** is one rank above beginner; **master** is two above beginner and one above advanced; **legendary** is the highest — three above beginner, two above advanced, one above master.

## Races

When you create your character, you must choose its race. A race does not give you major benefits — at most a minor combat perk, or a small boost to exploration and downtime. We recommend picking a race for its aesthetics, or because its concept truly connects with you (or, if you have no preference, whatever works best with your build).

### Human

The original mortal race — they still embody the unbreakable spirit that helped defeat the dead god.

- **Ability:** +1 unphased, and +1d8 to all willpower, intellect and charisma resist rolls.
- **Size:** 1 or 2.

### Elves

The shortest race of mortals in Drasil. Their nimble nature lends them to work as messengers between cities — a highly dangerous and respected job.

- **Ability:** +1d6 to all speed checks. When an elf *guards*, they add +1 to their evasion instead of gaining damage reduction.
- **Size:** 1.

### Ogres

Large mortals who recover from the most devastating wounds quickly — some legends tell of ogres who regrow lost limbs.

- **Ability:** +1 resilience, and **ogre's blood** — a spark of regeneration: whenever you regain 1 or more resilience from any source, regain 1 more.
- **Size:** 3.

### Rock gnomes

Mortals created by infusing elementals into empty shells of minerals. They are extremely rare.

- **Ability:** a permanent **−1 damage reduction** while wearing no armor, or light armor.
- **Size:** 1, 2 or 3.

### Devil-kin

Mortals infused with demonic essence at an early age. They may grow charcoal skin, and eyes the color of ice, fire or deep poisonous green.

- **Ability:** immune to continual fire, frost and poison damage — and gifted with **darkvision**: you see normally in darkness.
- **Size:** 2.

### Hybrids

Part human, part animal, these mortals adapted to life away from the big cities — small nomadic communities, settled wherever their animal side prefers.

A hybrid gains a feature based on their animal:

- **Primeape** — human and ape: +1 success on checks made to climb, and +1d10 to all attempts to impair a creature through restraining.
- **Bovine** — part cattle: bison, buffalo, yak. +2 resilience and +2 inventory space.
- **Canidae** — the kin of dogs, wolves and foxes: +1d10 to all sense checks based on smell or used to locate hidden things. When you make a hostile move against a creature within an ally's effective melee reach, gain +1d6 to the roll.
- **Feline** — part cat, jaguar, lion: your unarmed strikes gain the *bladed* trait — sharp claws and teeth — and you see perfectly well in the dark.
- **Aquatic** — the seafaring tribes, fish-human hybrids, most of them dangerous sea predators like sharks: a natural swimming speed equal to **double** your basic movement, and +2d10 to attacks, interception rolls and non-mental impairing attempts made underwater.
- **Anura** — a tribe of frog-like people: your jump distance increases by your speed dice size, and after you jump, you gain +1 evasion until you take damage or the round ends (doesn't stack across multiple jumps).

**Size:** 1, 2 or 3.

### Mound folk

A tribal culture with a special connection to the earth. Myths say they were created by the spirit of Teragamalt, the great tree.

- **Ability:** mound folk see through the ground itself. While blinded (and they may close their eyes at will to become blinded), they still perceive everything connected to the ground within a **15-meter radius**. This sight ignores invisibility (though not hidden enemies), and can be used to discover hidden paths and traps.
- **Size:** 1 or 2.

### Giant kin

Descendants of the union between mortals and giants, they inherited the giants' connection to arcane magic — and the craft of scribing ancient runes.

- **Ability:** a giant kin can **awaken** one object by scribing runes into it while polishing it during a rest. A weapon gains one of: *+1 damage and the fire tag*, *frost or electric tag*, or *mana drain*. A non-weapon object grants its wearer immunity to the effects of one damage tag (chosen at inscription). Only one object can be awakened at a time — awakening a new one ends the old.
- **Size:** 2 or 3.

### Seraphim

A seraphim is a person who bears the skin of an angel. Whether it was gifted, inborn, or grafted on in a ritual doesn't matter — what matters is the angel's ability to disguise its horrific appearance behind illusions.

A seraphim can appear as any gender and race — and even to the touch, they feel like the appearance they present. When a seraphim sleeps, they revert to their original, haunting visage.

- **Ability:** +1d10 to all non-spell-related charisma checks.
- **Size:** your original size is 3; you can shift your shape to any smaller size, down to 1.

### Wyvern Born

Bearers of the draconic spark — mortals infused with the innate potential of the first stage in the evolution of dragons. Most are born with the marks of that immortal touch: scales down the back, or draconic eyes with a piercing gaze. Their movements are swift, and the wind always seems to be at their side.

- **Ability:** slight control of the wind, like their originators. You learn the **air shield** and **gravity push** beginner spells — and once per round, you may cast *gravity push* at **+3 mana** to affect up to 5 creatures within the spell's range.
- **Size:** 1 or 2.

### Icewings

These people carry the power of the adult dragons that haunt the mountains of the world — a tribe that feeds on the flesh of dragons, or the result of a magical experiment with a dragon's essence. Cities are foreign to them: they are only at home among ice, wind and high places.

- **Ability:** a pair of wings that lets them **glide** — but not fly. An icewing cannot gain altitude by flying up, but uses the wings to ride their momentum downwards (see: [Flying](#flying)).
- The wings collectively count as a **poor quality spiked shield**: they can be raised, and strike back, as normal. Their quality grows with your endurance dice: 2 dice — standard, 3 — pristine, 4 — mastercraft. If the wings' resilience reaches 0, you cannot glide. While your wings are raised as shields, you gain no evasion benefits from other shields or their traits. The *heal up* activity restores **half** the wings' resilience.
- **Size:** 2.

### Stormblessed

Heirs of the divine sky dragons, who can bestow their awesome power upon a bloodline: the descendants of loyal followers slowly come to resemble their masters. On rare occasions, the power remains long after a family stopped serving its great dragon. Their appearance mirrors the storms that once ruled the heavens — electricity runs in their blood, and thunder seems to answer their call.

- **Ability:** completely immune to the **effects** of the *electric* damage tag (not to the damage itself — only its additional effects). You learn the **lightning burst** spell — and when you cast it, it deals only 1 damage to you.
- **Size:** 2 or 3.

### Racial modifications

Any race can be born with one of these — a mark that runs deeper than blood. A modification is optional, and it **adds to** your race's ability with something unique.

**Treeblessed** — those whose souls are bound to the world tree and the spirits of nature. Their bodies answer to the rhythms of the earth, and the forests themselves treat their presence with respect.

- When a treeblessed character is hit by a **fire** attack, it counts as being hit by an *armor destroyer* attack.
- A treeblessed creature is completely **immune to poisons**.

**Manifested** — their very essence is saturated with magic, showing through in even the simplest of actions. These beings seem woven from energy — and it answers their will faster than anyone else's. The mana in their body is so abundant it overrides their racial nature.

- Manifested start with **+3 starting mana**, and regain **double** the mana when meditating. Than Manifested affected by mana drain, they lose **three times** more mana, then another creatures.
